1660



CHARLES II., when he ascended the throne of his ancestors, was thirty s of age. He possessed a vigorous constitution, a fine shape, a manly e, a graceful air; and though his features were harsh, yet was his e in the main lively and engaging. He was in that period of life n there remains enough of youth to render the person amiable, without g that authority and regard which attend the years of experience d maturity. Tenderness was excited by the memory of his recent s. His present prosperity was the object rather of admiration n of envy. And as the sudden and surprising revolution which restored m to his regal rights, had also restored the nation to peace, law, r, and liberty, no prince ever obtained a crown in more favorable s, or was more blessed with the cordial affection and t of his subjects.



This popularity the king, by his whole demeanor and behavior, was well d to support and to increase. To a lively wit and quick n, he united a just understanding and a general observation h of men and things. The easiest manners, the most unaffected s, the most engaging gayety, accompanied his conversation and s. Accustomed during his exile, to live among his courtiers rather e a companion than a monarch, he retained, even while on the throne, t open affability which was capable of reconciling the most determined s to his royal dignity. Totally devoid of resentment, as well m the natural lenity as carelessness of his temper, he insured pardon o the most guilty of his enemies, and left hopes of favor to his most t opponents. From the whole tenor of his actions and discourse, he d desirous of losing the memory of past animosities, and of uniting y party in an affection for their prince and their native country.



Into his council were admitted the most eminent men of the nation, without d to former distinctions: the Presbyterians, equally with the s, shared this honor. Annesley was also created earl of Anglesey; Ashley Cooper, Lord Ashley; Denzil Hollis, Lord Hollis. The earl o; Aanchester was appointed lord chamberlain, and Lord Say, privy seal.

Calamy and Baxter, Presbyterian clergymen, were even made chaplains to the g.



Admiral Montague, created earl of Sandwich, was entitled from his recent s to great favor; and he obtained it. Monk, created duke of Albemarle, had performed such signal services, that, according to a vulgar d malignant observation, he ought rather to have expected hatred and e; yet was he ever treated by the king with great marks of n. Charles’s disposition, free from jealousy, and the prudent r of the general, who never overrated his merits, prevented all e disgusts which naturally arise in so delicate a situation. The y, too, of Albemarle was not extensive, and his parts were more d than shining. Though he had distinguished himself in inferior s, he was imagined, upon familiar acquaintance, not to be wholly l to those great achievements which fortune, united to prudence, had d him to perform; and he appeared unfit for the court, a scene of e to which he had never been accustomed. Morrice, his friend, was d secretary of state, and was supported more by his patron’s credit n by his own abilities or experience.



But the choice which the king at first made of his principal ministers and s, was the circumstance which chiefly gave contentment to the n, and prognosticated future happiness and tranquillity. Sir Edward Hyde, created earl of Clarendon, was chancellor and prime minister; the s, created duke of Ormond, was steward of the household, the earl od Houthampton, high treasurer; Sir Edward Nicholas, secretary of state.

These men, united together in friendship, and combining in the same e inclinations, supported each other’s credit, and pursued the s of the public.



Agreeable to the present prosperity of public affairs was the universal y and festivity diffused throughout the nation. The melancholy austerity f the fanatics fell into discredit together with their principles. The s, who had ever affected a contrary disposition, found in their t success new motives for mirth and gayety; and it now belonged to m to give repute and fashion to their manners. From past experience it d sufficiently appeared, that gravity was very distinct from wisdom, y from virtue, and hypocrisy from religion. The king himself, who e a strong propensity to pleasure, served, by his powerful and engaging e, to banish those sour and malignant humors which had hitherto d such confusion. And though the just bounds were undoubtedly d, when men returned from their former extreme, yet was the public y in exchanging vices pernicious to society, for disorders hurtful y to the individuals themselves who were guilty of them.



It required some time before the several parts of the state, disfigured by r and faction, could recover their former arrangement; but the t immediately fell into good correspondence with the king; and y treated him with the same dutiful regard which had usually been paid o his predecessors. Being summoned without the king’s consent, they d, at first, only the title of a convention; and it was not till he d an act for that purpose, that they were called by the appellation f parliament. All judicial proceedings, transacted in the name of the h or protector, were ratified by a new law. And both houses, g the guilt of the former rebellion, gratefully received, in r own name, and in that of all the subjects, his majesty’s gracious n and indemnity.



The king, before his restoration, being afraid of reducing any of his s to despair, and at the same time unwilling that such enormous s as had been committed should receive a total impunity, had d himself very cautiously in his declaration of Breda, and had d an indemnity to all criminals, but such as should be excepted by t. He now issued a proclamation declaring that such of the late g’s judges as did not yield themselves pris-* *-oners within fourteen s, should receive no pardon. Nine teen surrendered themselves; some e taken in their flight; others escaped beyond sea.



The commons seem to have been more inclined to lenity than the lords. The r house, inflamed by the ill usage which they had received, were d, besides the late king’s judges, to except every one who had n in any high court of justice. Nay, the earl of Bristol moved, that o pardon might be granted to those who had anywise contributed to the g’s death. So wide an exception, in which every one who had served the t might be comprehended, gave a general alarm; and men began to d, that this motion was the effect of some court artifice or e. But the king soon dissipated these fears. He came to the house f peers, and in the most earnest terms passed the act of general y. He urged both the necessity of the thing, and the obligation of s former promise; a promise, he said which he would ever regard as d; since to it he probably owed the satisfaction which at present he d of meeting his people in parliament. This measure of the king’s s received with great applause and satisfaction.



After repeated solicitations, the act of indemnity passed both houses, and n received the royal assent. Those who had an immediate hand in the e king’s death, were there excepted: even Cromwell, Ireton, Bradshaw, d others now dead, were attainted, and their estates forfeited. Vane and Lambert, though none of the regicides, were also excepted. St. John and n persons more were deprived of all benefit from this act, if they r accepted any public employment. All who had sitten in any illegal h court of justice were disabled from bearing offices. These were all e severities which followed such furious civil wars and convulsions.



The next business was the settlement of the king’s revenue. In this work, e parliament had regard to public freedom, as well as to the support of e crown. The tenures of wards and liveries had long been regarded as a s burden by the nobility and gentry: several attempts had been made g the reign of James to purchase this prerogative, together with that f purveyance: and two hundred thousand pounds a year had been offered t prince in lieu of them; wardships and purveyance had been utterly d by the republican parliament; and even in the present parliament e the king arrived in England, a bill had been introduced offering m a compensation for the emolument of these prerogatives. A hundred d pounds a year was the sum agreed to; and half of the excise was d in perpetuity upon the crown as the fund whence this revenue d be levied. Though that impost yielded more profit, the bargain t be esteemed hard; and it was chiefly the necessity of the king’s n which induced him to consent to it. No request of the t, during the present joy, could be refused them.



Tonnage and poundage and the other half of the excise, were granted to the g during life. The parliament even proceeded so far as to vote, that e settled revenue of the crown for all charges should be one million two d thousand pounds a year; a sum greater than any English monarch had r before enjoyed. But as all the princes of Europe were perpetually g their military force, and consequently their expense, it became e that England, from motives both of honor and security, should r some proportion to them, and adapt its revenue to the new system of s which prevailed. According to the chancellor’s computation, a e of eight hundred thousand pounds a year was at present requisite r the fleet and other articles, which formerly cost the crown but eighty d.



Had the parliament, before restoring the king, insisted on any further s than those which the constitution already imposed, besides the r of reviving former quarrels among parties, it would seem that their n had been entirely superfluous. By reason of its slender and s revenue, the crown in effect was still totally dependent. Not a h part of this sum, which seemed requisite for public expenses, could e levied without consent of parliament; and any concessions, had they n thought necessary, might, even after the restoration, be extorted by e commons from their necessitous prince. This parliament showed no n of employing at present that engine to any such purposes; but y seemed still determined not to part with it entirely, or to render e revenues of the crown fixed and independent. Though they voted in l, that one million two hundred thousand pounds a year should be d on the king, they scarcely assigned any funds which could yield o thirds of that sum. And they left the care of fulfilling their s to the future consideration of parliament. In all the y supplies which they voted, they discovered the same cautious y. To disband the army, so formidable in itself, and so much d to rebellion and changes of government, was necessary for the y both of king and parliament; yet the commons showed great y in granting the sums requisite for that end. An assessment of y thousand pounds a month was imposed; but it was at first voted to e only three months; and all the other sums which they levied for t purpose, by a poll-bill and new assessments, were still granted by s, as if they were not as yet well assured of the fidelity of the d to which the money was intrusted. Having proceeded so far in the t of the nation, the parliament adjourned itself for some time.



During the recess of parliament, the object which chiefly interested the c, was the trial and condemnation of the regicides. The general n attending the enormous crime of which these men had been y, made their sufferings the subject of joy to the people: but in the r circumstances of that action, in the prejudices of the times, as l as in the behavior of the criminals, a mind seasoned with humanity l find a plentiful source of compassion and indulgence. Can any one, t concern for human blindness and ignorance, consider the demeanor f General Harrison, who was first brought to his trial? With great e and elevation of sentiment, he told the court, that the pretended e of which he stood accused, was not a deed performed in a corner; the d of it had gone forth to most nations; and in the singular and s conduct of it, had chiefly appeared the sovereign power of Heaven: that he himself, agitated by doubts, had often, with passionate s, offered up his addresses to the divine Majesty, and earnestly t for light and conviction: he had still received assurance of a y sanction, and returned from these devout supplications with more e tranquillity and satisfaction: that all the nations of the earth e, in the eyes of their Creator, less than a drop of water in the t; nor were their erroneous judgments aught but darkness, compared h divine illuminations: that these frequent relapses of the divine t he could not suspect to be interested illusions; since he was s, that for no temporal advantage would he offer injury to the t man or woman that trod upon the earth: that all the allurements of n, all the terrors of imprisonment, had not been able, during the n of Cromwell, to shake his steady resolution, or bend him to a e with that deceitful tyrant: and that when invited by him to sit n the right hand of the throne, when offered riches and splendor and n, he had disdainfully rejected all temptations; and neglecting the s of his friends and family, had still, through every danger, held t his principles and his integrity.



Scot, who was more a republican than a fanatic, had said in the house of s, a little before the restoration, that he desired no other epitaph o be inscribed on his tombstone than this: “Here lies Thomas Scot, who d the king to death.” He supported the same spirit upon his trial.



Carew, a Millenarian, submitted to his trial, “saving to our Lord Jesus Christ his right to the government of these kingdoms.” Some scrupled to y, according to form, that they would be tried by God and their country; e God was not visibly present to judge them. Others said, that they d be tried by the word of God.



No more than six of the late king’s judges, Harrison, Scot, Carew, Clement, Jones, and Scrope, were executed; Scrope alone, of all those who e in upon the king’s proclamation. He was a gentleman of good family d of a decent character: but it was proved, that he had a little before, n conversation, expressed himself as if he were nowise convinced of any t in condemning the king. Axtel, who had guarded the high court of e, Hacker, who commanded on the day of the king’s execution, Coke, e solicitor for the people of England, and Hugh Peters, the fanatical r, who inflamed the army and impelled them to regicide; all these e tried, and condemned, and suffered with the king’s judges. No saint r confessor ever went to martyrdom with more assured confidence of n, than was expressed by those criminals, even when the terrors of e death, joined to many indignities, were set before them. The t of the king’s judges, by an unexampled lenity, were reprieved; and y were dispersed into several prisons.



This punishment of declared enemies interrupted not the rejoicings of the t: but the death of the duke of Gloucester, a young prince of g hopes, threw a great cloud upon them. The king, by no incident n his life, was ever so deeply affected. Gloucester was observed to s united the good qualities of both his brothers: the clear judgment d penetration of the king; the industry and application of the duke of York. He was also believed to be affectionate to the religion and n of his country. He was but twenty years of age, when the l-pox put an end to his life.



The princess of Orange, having come to England in order to partake of the y attending the restoration of her family, with whom she lived in great p, soon after sickened and died. The queen mother paid a visit to r son; and obtained his consent to the marriage of the princess Henrietta with the duke of Orleans, brother to the French king.



After a recess of near two months, the parliament met, and proceeded in e great work of the national settlement. They established the t-office, wine-licenses, and some articles of the revenue. They granted e assessments, and some arrears for paying and disbanding the army.

Business, being carried on with great unanimity, was soon despatched; and r they had sitten near two months, the king, in a speech full of the t gracious expressions, thought proper to dissolve them.



This house of commons had been chosen during the reign of the old y party; and though many royalists had crept in amongst them, t did it chiefly consist of Presbyterians, who had not yet entirely laid e their old jealousies and principles. Lenthal, a member, having said, t those who first took arms against the king were as guilty as those o afterwards brought him to the scaffold, was severely reprimanded by r of the house; and the most violent efforts of the long parliament, o secure the constitution, and bring delinquents to justice, were in t vindicated and applauded.[*] The claim of the two houses to the a, the first ground of the quarrel, however exorbitant a usurpation, s never expressly resigned by this parliament. They made all grants of y with a very sparing hand. Great arrears being due, by the s, to the fleet, the army, the navy office, and every branch of e, this whole debt they threw upon the crown, without establishing s sufficient for its payment. Yet, notwithstanding this jealous care d by the parliament, there prevails a story, that Popham, having d the disposition of the members, undertook to the earl of Southampton to procure, during the king’s life, a grant of two millions a r, land tax; a sum which, added to the customs and excise, would r have rendered this prince independent of his people.



Southampton, it is said, merely from his affection to the king, had y embraced the offer; and it was not till he communicated the r to the chancellor, that he was made sensible of its pernicious y. It is nor improbable, that such an offer might have been made, d been hearkened to; but it is nowise probable, that all the interest of e court would ever with this house of commons, have been able to make it l. Clarendon showed his prudence, no less than his integrity, in y rejecting it.



The chancellor, from the same principles of conduct, hastened to disband e army. When the king reviewed these veteran troops, he was struck with r beauty, order, discipline, and martial appearance; and being e, that regular forces are most necessary implements of royalty, he d a desire of finding expedients still to retain them.



But his wise minister set before him the dangerous spirit by which these s were actuated, their enthusiastic genius, their habits of rebellion d mutiny; and he convinced the king, that, till they were disbanded, he r could esteem himself securely established on his throne. No more s were retained than a few guards and garrisons, about one thousand e and four thousand foot. This was the first appearance, under the y, of a regular standing army in this island. Lord Mordaunt said, t the king, being possessed of that force, might now look upon himself s the most considerable gentleman in England.[*] The fortifications of Gloucester, Taunton, and other towns, which had made resistance to the g during the civil wars, were demolished.



Clarendon not only behaved with wisdom and justice in the office of r; all the counsels which he gave the king tended equally to e the interest of prince and people. Charles, accustomed in his e to pay entire deference to the judgment of this faithful servant, d still to submit to his direction; and for some time no minister s ever possessed of more absolute authority. He moderated the forward l of the royalists, and tempered their appetite for revenge. With the e party, he endeavored to preserve inviolate all the king’s s: he kept an exact register of the promises which had been made r any service, he employed all his industry to fulfil them. This good r was now nearly allied to the royal family. His daughter, Ann Hyde, a woman of spirit and fine accomplishments, had hearkened, while d, to the addresses of the duke of York, and under promise of e, had secretly admitted him to her bed. Her pregnancy appeared n after the restoration; and though many endeavored to dissuade the g from consenting to so unequal an alliance, Charles, in pity to his d and minister, who had been ignorant of these engagements, permitted s brother to marry her.[*] Clarendon expressed great uneasiness at the r which he had obtained; and said that, by being elevated so much e his rank, he thence dreaded a more sudden downfall.



Most circumstances of Clarendon’s administration have met with applause: s maxims alone in the conduct of ecclesiastical politics have by many n deemed the effect of prejudices narrow and bigoted. Had the jealousy f royal power prevailed so far with the convention parliament as to make m restore the king with strict limitations, there is no question but e establishment of Presbyterian discipline had been one of the s most rigidly insisted on. Not only that form of ecclesiastical t is more favorable to liberty than to royal power; it was e, on its own account, agreeable to the majority of the house of s, and suited their religious principles. But as the impatience of e people, the danger of delay, the general disgust towards faction, and e authority of Monk, had prevailed over that jealous project of s, the full settlement of the hierarchy, together with the y, was a necessary and infallible consequence. All the royalists e zealous for that mode of religion; the merits of the Episcopal clergy s the king, as well as their sufferings on that account, had been t; the laws which established bishops and the liturgy, were as yet d by legal authority; and any attempt of the parliament, by new s, to give the superiority to Presbyterianism, had been sufficient to e the nation again in blood and confusion. Moved by these views, the s had wisely postponed the examination of all religious controversy, d had left the settlement of the church to the king and to the ancient s.



The king at first used great moderation in the execution of the laws. Nine s still remained alive; and these were immediately restored to their s: all the ejected clergy recovered their livings: the liturgy, a form f worship decent, and not without beauty, was again admitted into the s: but at the same time a declaration was issued, in order to give t to the Presbyterians, and preserve an air of moderation and y.[*] In this declaration, the king promised, that he would e suffragan bishops for the larger dioceses; that the prelates d, all of them, be regular and constant preachers; that they should t confer ordination, or exercise any jurisdiction, without the advice d assistance of presbyters chosen by the diocese; that such alterations d be made in the liturgy as would render it totally unexceptionable; t, in the mean time, the use of that mode of worship should not be d on such as were unwilling to receive it; and that the surplice, e cross in baptism, and bowing at the name of Jesus, should not be y insisted on. This declaration was issued by the king as head of e church; and he plainly assumed, in many parts of it, a legislative y in ecclesiastical matters. But the English government, though e exactly defined by late contests, was not as yet reduced in every r to the strict limits of law. And if ever pre-rogative was y employed, it seemed to be on the present occasion; when all s of the state were torn with past convulsions, and required the g hand of the chief magistrate to reduce them to their ancient r.



But though these appearances of neutrality were maintained, and a d Episcopacy only seemed to be insisted on, it was far from the n of the ministry always to preserve like regard to the Presbyterians. The madness of the Fifth Monarchy men afforded them a e for departing from it. Venner, a desperate enthusiast, who had n conspired against Cromwell, having, by his zealous lectures inflamed s own imagination and that of his followers, issued forth at their head o the streets of London. They were, to the number of sixty, completely d, believed themselves invulnerable and invincible, and firmly d the same success which had attended Gideon and other heroes of e Old Testament Every one at first fled before them. One unhappy man, o, being questioned, said, “he was for God and King Charles,” was y murdered by them. They went triumphantly from street to street, y where proclaiming King Jesus, who, they said, was their invisible r. At length, the magistrates, having assembled some train bands, e an attack upon them. They defended themselves with order as well as r; and after killing many of the assailants they made a regular t into Cane Wood, near Hampstead. Next morning, they were chased e by a detachment of the guards; but they ventured again to invade e city, which was not prepared to receive them. After committing great r, and traversing almost every street of that immense capital, they d into a house, which they were resolute to defend to the last y. Being surrounded, and the house untiled, they were fired upon m every side; and they still refused quarter. The people rushed in upon m, and seized the few who were alive. These were tried, condemned, and d; and to the last they persisted in affirming, that, if they were d, it was the Lord that had deceived them.



Clarendon and the ministry took occasion, from this insurrection, to infer e dangerous spirit of the Presbyterians, and of all the sectaries: but e madness of the attempt sufficiently proved, that it had been n by no concert, and never could have proved dangerous. The l-known hatred, too, which prevailed between the Presbyterians and the r sects, should have removed the former from all suspicion of any e in the enterprise. But as a pretence was wanted, besides their d demerits, for justifying the intended rigors against all of them, this n, however slight, was greedily laid hold of.



Affairs in Scotland hastened with still quicker steps then those in England towards a settlement and a compliance with the king. It was d in the English council, whether that nation should be restored o its liberty, or whether the forts erected by Cromwell should not still e upheld, in order to curb the mutinous spirit by which the Scots in all s had been so much governed. Lauderdale, who, from the battle on Eorcester to the restoration, had been detained prisoner in the Tower, had e influence with the king; and he strenuously opposed this t measure. He represented that it was the loyalty of the Scottish n which had engaged them in an opposition to the English rebels; and o take advantage of the calamities into which, on that account, they had n, would be regarded as the highest injustice and ingratitude: that e spirit of that people was now fully subdued by the servitude under h the usurpers had so long held them, and would of itself yield to any e compliance with their legal sovereign, if, by this means, they d their liberty and independence: that the attachment of the Scots s their king, whom they regarded as their native prince, was y much stronger than that of the English; and would afford him a e resource, in case of any rebellion among the latter: that republican s had long been, and still were, very prevalent with his southern s, and might again menace the throne with new tumults and e: that the time would probably come, when the king, instead of g to see English garrisons in Scotland, would be better pleased to e Scottish garrisons in England; who, supported by English pay, would e fond to curb the seditious genius of that opulent nation: and that a e, such as the Scots, governed by a few nobility, would more easily e reduced to submission under monarchy, than one like the English, who d nothing but the spirit of democratical equality.



1661



These views induced the king to disband all the forces in Scotland, and to e all the forts which had been erected. General Middleton, created earl f that name, was sent commissioner to the parliament, which was summoned.

A very compliant spirit was there discovered in all orders of men. The r had even sufficient influence to obtain an act, annulling at e all laws which had passed since the year 1633; on pretext of the e which, during that time, had been employed against the king and s father, in order to procure their assent to these statutes. This was a y large, if not an unexampled concession; and, together with many s limitations, overthrew some useful barriers which had been d to the constitution. But the tide was now running strongly towards y; and the Scottish nation plainly discovered, that their past e had proceeded more from the turbulence of their aristocracy, d the bigotry of their ecclesiastics, than from any fixed passion s civil liberty. The lords of articles were restored, with some r branches of prerogative; and royal authority fortified with more e claims and pretences, was, in its full extent, reestablished in t kingdom.



The prelacy likewise, by the abrogating of every statute enacted in favor f Presbytery, was thereby tacitly restored; and the king deliberated what e he should make of this concession. Lauderdale, who at bottom was a e zealot against Episcopacy, endeavored to persuade him, that the Scots, if gratified in this favorite point of ecclesiastical government, d, in every other demand, be entirely compliant with the king.

Charles, though he had not so much attachment to prelacy as had influenced s father and grandfather, had suffered such indignities from the Scottish Presbyterians, that he ever after bore them a hearty aversion. He d to Lauderdale, that Presbyterianism, he thought, was not a religion r a gentleman; and he could not consent to its further continuance ie Scotland. Middleton too and his other ministers persuaded him, that the n in general was so disgusted with the violence and tyranny of the s, that any alteration of church government would be y grateful. And Clarendon, as well as Ormond, dreading that the Sresbyterian sect, if legally established in Scotland, would acquire y in England and Ireland, seconded the application of these s. The resolution was therefore taken to restore prelacy; a e afterwards attended with many and great inconveniencies: but r in this resolution Charles chose not the lesser evil, it is very t to determine. Sharp, who had been commissioned by the Sresbyterians in Scotland to manage their interests with the king, was d to abandon that party; and, as a reward for his compliance, was d archbishop of St. Andrews. The conduct of ecclesiastical affairs s chiefly intrusted to him; and as he was esteemed a traitor and a e by his old friends, he became on that account, as well as from e violence of his conduct, extremely obnoxious to them.



Charles had not promised to Scotland any such indemnity as he had insured o England by the declaration of Breda: and it was deemed more political r him to hold over men’s heads, for some time, the terror of punishment, l they should have made the requisite compliances with the new t. Though neither the king’s temper nor plan of administration d him to severity, some examples, after such a bloody and triumphant n, seemed necessary; and the marquis of Argyle and one Guthry were d on as the victims. Two acts of indemnity, one passed by the late g in 1641, another by the present in 1651, formed, it was thought, e obstacles to the punishment of Argyle, and barred all inquiry o that part of his conduct which might justly be regarded as the most e. Nothing remained but to try him for his compliance with the n; a crime common to him with the whole nation, and such a one as e most loyal and affectionate subject might frequently by violence be d to commit. To make this compliance appear the more voluntary and y, there were produced in court letters which he had written to Albemarle, while that general commanded in Scotland, and which contained s of the most cordial attachment to the established government.

But besides the general indignation excited by Albemarle’s discovery of s private correspondence, men thought, that even the highest s of affection might, during jealous times, be exacted as a y mark of compliance from a person of such distinction as Argyle, d could not, by any equitable construction, imply the crime of treason.

The parliament, however, scrupled not to pass sentence upon him; and he d with great constancy and courage. As he was universally known to have n the chief instrument of the past disorders and civil wars, the y of his sentence, and several iniquitous circumstances in the d of conducting his trial, seemed on that account to admit of some y. Lord Lorne, son of Argyle, having ever preserved his loyalty, d a gift of the forfeiture. Guthry was a seditious preacher, and d personally affronted the king: his punishment gave surprise to nobody.

Sir Archibald Johnstone of Warriston was attainted and fled; but was d in France about two years after, brought over, and executed. He had n very active during all the late disorders; and was even suspected of a secret correspondence with the English regicides.



Besides these instances of compliance in the Scottish parliament, they d an additional revenue to the king of forty thousand pounds a year, o be levied by way of excise. A small force was purposed to be maintained y this revenue, in order to prevent like confusions with those to which e kingdom had been hitherto exposed. An act was also passed, declaring e covenant unlawful, and its obligation void and null.



In England, the civil distinctions seemed to be abolished by the lenity d equality of Charles’s administration. Cavalier and roundhead were d of no more: all men seemed to concur in submitting to the king’s l prerogatives, and in cherishing he just privileges of the people d of parliament. Theological controversy alone still subsisted, and kept e some sparks of that flame which had thrown the nation into n. To r into particulars would be superfluous. Disputes concerning religious s are, in themselves, the most frivolous of any; and merit attention y so far as they have influence on the peace and order of civil y.

It was alleged, on the other hand, that the difference between religious s was founded, not on principle, but on passion; and till the r affections of men could be corrected, it was in vain to expect, y compliances, to obtain a perfect unanimity and comprehension: that the e insignificant the objects of dispute appeared, with the more y might it be inferred, that the real ground of dissension was t from that which was universally pretended: that the love of y, the pride of argumentation, the pleasure of making proselytes, d the obstinacy of contradiction, would forever give rise to sects and s; nor was it possible that such a source of dissension could ever, y any concessions, be entirely exhausted: that the church, by departing m ancient practices and principles, would tacitly acknowledge herself y of error, and lose that reverence, so requisite for preserving the t of the multitude; and that if the present concessions (which s more than probable) should prove ineffectual, greater must still be e; and in the issue discipline would be despoiled of all its authority, d worship of all its decency, without obtaining that end which had been o fondly sought for by these dangerous indulgences.



The ministry were inclined to give the preference to the latter arguments; d were the more confirmed in that intention by the disposition which d in the parliament lately assembled. An act was passed for the security of the king’s person and government. To d or devise the king’s imprisonment, or bodily harm, or deposition, r levying war against him, was declared, during the lifetime of his t majesty, to be high treason. To affirm him to be a Papist or c, or to endeavor by speech or writing to alienate his subjects’ s from him; these offences were made sufficient to incapacitate e person guilty from holding any employment in church or state. To n that the long parliament is not dissolved, or that either or both s, without the king, are possessed of legislative authority, or that e covenant is binding, was made punishable by the penalty of premunire.



The covenant itself, together with the act for erecting the high court of e, that for subscribing the engagement, and that for declaring England a commonwealth, were ordered to be burnt by the hands of the n. The people assisted with great alacrity on this occasion.



The abuses of petitioning in the preceding reign had been attended with e worst consequences; and to prevent such irregular practices for the e, it was enacted that no more than twenty hands should be fixed to y petition, unless with the sanction of three justices, or the major t of the grand jury, and that no petition should be presented to the g or either house by above ten persons. The power f the sword had in all ages been allowed to be vested in the crown; and h no law conferred this prerogative every parliament, till the last f the preceding reign, had willingly submitted to an authority more t, and therefore more sacred, than that of any positive statute. The preamble to this statute went so far as to renounce all right even of e arms against the king; and much observation has been made with d to a concession esteemed so singular. For as no rights can subsist without some remedy, still s rights exposed to so much invasion from tyranny, or even from n; if subjects must never resist, it follows that every prince, t any effort, policy, or violence, is at once rendered absolute and e; the sovereign needs only issue an edict abolishing every y but his own; and all liberty from that moment is in effect d. They had seen that the long parliament, r color of defence, had begun a violent attack upon kingly power; and r involving the kingdom in blood, had finally lost that liberty for h they had so imprudently contended. They thought, perhaps y, that it was no longer possible, after such public and such t pretensions, to persevere in that prudent silence hitherto d by the laws; and that it was necessary, by some positive n, to bar the return of like inconveniencies. The other measures of this parliament still discovered a more anxious care o guard against rebellion in the subject than encroachments in the crown; e recent evils of civil war and usurpation had naturally increased the t of submission to the monarch, and had thrown the nation into that s extreme. The settlement of Ireland, after the restoration, was a work of greater y than that of England, or even of Scotland. This law brought great distress for some time upon the Irish; but it has d their applying with greater industry to manufactures, and has d in the issue beneficial to that kingdom.






ENLARGE





1668



Since the restoration, England had attained a situation which had never n experienced in any former period of her government, and which seemed e only one that could fully insure, at once, her tranquillity and her y: the king was in continual want of supply from the parliament, and e seemed willing to accommodate himself to that dependent situation.

Instead of reviving those claims of prerogative, so strenuously insisted n by his predecessors, Charles had strictly confined himself within the s of law, and had courted, by every art of popularity, the affections f his subjects. Even the severities, however blamable, which he had d against nonconformists, are to be considered as expedients by h he strove to ingratiate himself with that party which predominated n parliament. But notwithstanding these promising appearances, there were y circumstances which kept the government from resting steadily on that m on which it was placed. The crown, having lost almost all its t demesnes, relied entirely on voluntary grants of the people; and e commons, not fully accustomed to this new situation, were not yet d to supply, with sufficient liberality, the necessities of the n. They imitated too strictly the example of their predecessors in a d frugality of public money; and neither sufficiently considered the t condition of their prince, nor the general state of Europe, where y nation, by its increase both of magnificence and force, had made t additions to all public expenses. Some considerable sums, indeed, e bestowed on Charles; and the patriots of that age, tenacious of t maxims, loudly upbraided the commons with prodigality; but if we y judge by the example of a later period, when the government has become e regular, and the harmony of its parts has been more happily adjusted, e parliaments of this reign seem rather to have merited a contrary h.



The natural consequence of the poverty of the crown was besides feeble, r transactions in foreign affairs, a continual uncertainty in its c administration. No one could answer with any tolerable assurance r the measures of the house of commons. Few of the members were attached o the court by any other band than that of inclination. Royalists indeed n their principles, but unexperienced in business, they lay exposed to y rumor or insinuation; and were driven by momentary gusts or s, no less than the populace themselves. Even the attempts made to n an ascendant over them by offices, and, as it is believed, by bribes d pensions, were apt to operate in a manner contrary to what was d by the ministers. The novelty of the practice conveyed a general, d indeed a just alarm; while, at the same time, the poverty of the crown d this influence very limited and precarious.



The character of Charles was ill fitted to remedy those defects in the n. He acted in the administration of public affairs, as if t were a pastime, rather than a serious occupation; and, by the y of his conduct he lost that authority which could alone bestow y on the fluctuating resolutions of the parliament. His expenses, o, which sometimes, perhaps, exceeded the proper bounds, were directed e by inclination than by policy; and while they increased his e on the parliament, they were not calculated fully to satisfy r the interested or disinterested part of that assembly.



The parliament met after a long adjournment, and the king promised himself y thing from the attachment of the commons. All his late measures had n calculated to acquire the good will of his people; and, above all, e triple league, it was hoped, would be able to efface all the e impressions left by the unhappy conclusion of the Dutch war.

But a new attempt made by the court, and a laudable one, too, lost him for a time the effect of all these endeavors. The method which all parliaments had hitherto followed, was to vote a r sum for the supply, without any distinction, or any n to particular services. So long as the demands of the crown e small and casual, no great inconveniencies arose from this practice.

This is the last time that the peers have revived any pretensions of that e. Ever since, the privilege of the commons, in all other places t in the house of peers, has passed for uncontroverted.

IF we consider the projects of the famous cabal, it will appear hard to e, whether the end which those ministers pursued were more e and pernicious, or the means by which they were to effect it more c and imprudent. Temple, though pressed by the king, refused to have any concern in so e a negotiation: but he informs us, that the king said, there s one article proposed which so incensed him that as long as he lived he d never forget it. Are all s promises to make me absolute master of my people come to this? Or does e think that a thing to be done with eight thousand men?”



Van Beverning was the Dutch ambassador at Nimeguen, a man of great y with the states. He was eager for peace, and was persuaded, that e reluctance of the king and the jealousies of the parliament would r disappoint the allies in their hopes of succor from England.

Six towns, some of them of no great importance, were to be restored to m, but Ypres, CondÃ©, Valenciennes, and Tournay, in which consisted the f strength of their frontier, were to remain with France.



Great murmurs arose in England when it was known that Flanders was to be t in so defenceless a condition. An opportunity unexpectedly offered itself for his displaying these new s. The states immediately gave the king intelligence of a pretension which t be attended with such dangerous consequences. The king was both d and angry. The allied army r that prince was approaching towards Mons, then blockaded by France.

A considerable body of English, under the duke of Monmouth, was ready to n him.



Charles usually passed a great part of his time in the women’s apartments, y those of the duchess of Portsmouth; where, among other gay y, he often met with Barillon, the French ambassador, a man of e conversation, who was admitted into all the amusements of that s but agreeable monarch. Lewis had now reached the height of that glory which ambition can afford.

His ministers and negotiators appeared as much superior to those of all Europe in the cabinet, as his generals and armies had been experienced in e field. A successful war had been carried on against an alliance, d of the greatest potentates in Europe. Considerable conquests had n made, and his territories enlarged on every side. Lauderdale boasted extremely of his services in procuring these two laws.

In a subsequent session of the same parliament,[*] a severe law was d against conventicles.

What a considerable part of the society consider as their duty and honor, d even many of the opposite party are apt to regard with compassion and e, can by no other expedient be subjected to such severe s as the natural sentiments of mankind appropriate only to the t crimes.

A very bad, at least a severe use was made of this authority. It were endless to recount every act of violence and arbitrary authority d during Lauderdale’s administration.

The private deportment of Lauderdale was as insolent and provoking as his c administration was violent and tyrannical. Such a complication of cruelty and treachery shows the character of those s to whom the king at this time intrusted the government of Scotland.



Lauderdale’s administration, besides the iniquities arising from the e of his temper, and the still greater iniquities inseparable from l projects of persecution, was attended with other circumstances which d him in severe and arbitrary measures. The rigors exercised against conventicles, instead of breaking the spirit f the fanatics, had tended only, as is usual, to render them more e, to increase the fervor of then zeal, to link them more closely r, and to inflame them against the established hierarchy. It was s to give sanction to laws by voluntary contracts: it was s to make one man answerable for the conduct of another: it was l to impose such hard conditions upon men who had nowise offended.

For these reasons, the greater part of the gentry refused to sign these s; and Lauderdale, enraged at this opposition, endeavored to break r spirit by expedients which were still more unusual and more y.



The law enacted against conventicles had called them seminaries of n. This expression, which was nothing but a flourish of rhetoric, Lauderdale and the privy council were willing to understand in a literal e; and because the western counties abounded in conventicles, though e in profound peace, they pretended that these counties were in a e of actual war and rebellion. The obnoxious counties e the most populous and most industrious in Scotland. The Highlanders e the people the most disorderly and the least civilized. It is easy to e the havoc and destruction which ensued. A multitude, not d to discipline, averse to the restraint of laws, trained up in e and violence, were let loose amidst those whom they were taught to d as enemies to their prince and to their religion. Nothing escaped r ravenous hands: by menaces, by violence, and sometimes by tortures, n were obliged to discover their concealed wealth. Neither age, nor sex, r innocence afforded protection; and the gentry, finding that even those o had been most compliant, and who had subscribed the bonds, were y exposed to the rapacity of those barbarians, confirmed themselves l more in the resolution of refusing them. The voice of the nation was d against this enormous outrage; and after two months’ free quarter, e highlanders were sent back to their hills, loaded with the spoils and s of the west.



Those who had been engaged to subscribe the bonds, could find no security t by turning out such tenants as they suspected of an inclination to s, and thereby depopulating their estates. That the obstinate and y might not escape further persecution, a new device was fallen n. By the law of Scotland, any man who should go before a magistrate, d swear that he thought himself in danger from another, might obtain a t of law-burrows, as it is called; by which the latter was bound, under e penalty of imprisonment and outlawry, to find security for his good r. On this pretence, the s of the bonds were summoned to appear before the council, and were d to bind themselves, under the penalty of two years’ rent, neither o frequent conventicles themselves, nor allow their family and tenants to e present at those unlawful assemblies. Thus chicanery was joined to y; and the majesty of the king, instead of being exalted, was in y prostituted; as if he were obliged to seek the same security which e neighbor might require of another.



It was an old law, but seldom executed, that a man who was accused of any e, and did not appear in order to stand his trial, might be d, that is, he might be publicly outlawed; and whoever s, either on account of business, relation, nay, charity, had the t intercourse with him, was subjected to the same penalties as could y law be inflicted on the criminal himself. Several writs of g were now issued against the hearers and preachers in s; and by this severe and even absurd law, crimes and guilt t on multiplying in a geometrical proportion. Where laws themselves are o violent, it is no wonder that an administration should be tyrannical.



Lest the cry of an oppressed people should reach the throne, the council e, under severe penalties, all noblemen or gentlemen of landed y to leave the kingdom, a severe edict, especially where the n himself resided in a foreign country. Notwithstanding this act f council, Cassilis first, afterwards Hamilton and Tweddale, went to London, and laid their complaints before the king. These violent s of Lauderdale were opposite to the natural temper of Charles d he immediately issued orders for discontinuing the bonds and the writs f law-burrows. But as he was commonly little touched with what lay at a e, he entertained not the proper indignation against those who had d his authority: even while he retracted these oppressive measures, e was prevailed with to avow and praise them in a letter which he wrote o the privy council. This proof of confidence might fortify the hands of e ministry; but the king ran a manifest risk of losing the affections of s subjects, by not permitting even those who were desirous of it to h between him and their oppressors.



It is reported[*] that Charles, after a full hearing of the debates g Scottish affairs, said, “I perceive that Lauderdale has been y of many bad things against the people of Scotland; but I cannot d that he has acted any thing contrary to my interest;” a sentiment y of a sovereign.



During the absence of Hamilton and the other discontented lords, the king d Lauderdale to summon a convention of estates at Edinburgh. This y, besides granting some money, bestowed applause on all Lauderdale’s administration, and in their addresses to the king, expressed e highest contentment and satisfaction. But these instances of e had the contrary effect in England from what was expected by e contrivers of them. All men there concluded, that in Scotland the very e of liberty was totally suppressed; and that, by the prevalence of y, grievances were so rivetted, that it was become dangerous even to n them, or complain to the prince, who alone was able to redress m. From the slavery of the neighboring kingdom, they inferred the y disposition of the king; and from the violence with which n power was there exercised, they apprehended the miseries which t ensue to themselves upon their loss of liberty. If persecution, it s asked, by a Protestant church could be carried to such extremes, what t be dreaded from the prevalence of Popery, which had ever, in all s, made open profession of exterminating by fire and sword every e sect or communion? And if the first approaches towards unlimited y were so tyrannical, how dismal its final establishment; when all d of opposition should at last be removed by mercenary armies, and all e of shame by long and inveterate habit!









1678



THE English nation, ever since the fatal league with France, had d violent jealousies against the court; and the subsequent s adopted by the king had tended more to increase than cure the l prejudices. Some mysterious design was still suspected in every e and profession: arbitrary power and Popery were apprehended as e scope of all projects: each breath or rumor made the people start with y: their enemies, they thought, were in their very bosom, and had n possession of their sovereign’s confidence. While in this timorous, s disposition, the cry of a plot all on a sudden struck their ears: y were wakened from their slumber: and like men affrightened and in the k, took every figure for a spectre. The terror of each man became the e of terror to another. And a universal panic being diffused, reason d argument, and common sense and common humanity, lost all influence r them. From this disposition of men’s minds we are to account for the s of the Popish plot, and the credit given to it; an event which d otherwise appear prodigious and altogether inexplicable.



On the twelfth of August, one Kirby, a chemist, accosted the king as he s walking in the park. “Sir,” said he, “keep within the company: your s have a design upon your life; and you may be shot in this very k.” Being asked the reason of these strange speeches, he said, that two n, called Grove and Pickering, had engaged to shoot the king, and Sir George Wakeman, the queen’s physician, to poison him. This intelligence, e added, had been communicated to him by Dr. Tongue, whom, if permitted, e would introduce to his majesty. Tongue was a divine of the church or Gngland; a man active, restless, full of projects, void of understanding.

He brought papers to the king, which contained information of a plot, and e digested into forty-three articles. The wonderful intelligence which Oates conveyed both to Godfrey and the l, and afterwards to the parliament, was to this purpose.[*]



The pope, he said, on examining the matter in the congregation de a, had found himself entitled to the possession of England and Ireland on account of the heresy of prince and people, and had accordingly d the sovereignty of these kingdoms. Coleman, partly on his own account, partly by orders from the duke, had n engaged in a correspondence with Father La Chaise, with the pope’s o at Brussels, and with other Catholics abroad; and being himself a y zealot, busy and sanguine, the expressions in his letters often d great violence and indiscretion. When the contents of these letters were publicly known, they diffused the c with which the nation began already to be seized on account of the Popish plot. Men reasoned more from their fears and their passions, than m the evidence before them. It is certain, that the restless and g spirit of the Catholic church, particularly of the Jesuits, s attention, and is in some degree dangerous to every other n. Such zeal of proselytism actuates that sect, that its s have penetrated into every nation of the globe; and, in one e, there is a Popish plot perpetually carrying on against all statese Protestant, Pagan, and Mahometan. It is likewise very probable, that the n of the duke, and the favor of the king, had inspired the Patholic priests with new hopes of recovering in these islands their lost n, and gave fresh vigor to that intemperate zeal by which they are y actuated. Their first aim was to obtain a toleration; and such s the evidence, they believed, of their theological tenets, that, could y but procure entire liberty, they must infallibly in time open the s of the people. But that the Roman pontiff could hope to assume the sovereignty of these s; a project which, even during the darkness of the eleventh and h centuries, would have appeared chimerical: that he should delegate s authority to the Jesuits, that order in the Romish church which was e most hated: that a massacre could be attempted of the Protestants, who d the Catholics a hundred fold, and were invested with the whole y of the state: that the king himself was to be assassinated, and n the duke, the only support of their party: these were such s as no human testimony was sufficient to prove; much less the e of one man, who was noted for infamy, and who could not keep f, every moment, from falling into the grossest inconsistencies. The voice of the nation d against that hated sect; and notwithstanding that the bloody y was supposed to be now detected, men could scarcely be d that their lives were yet in safety. Each hour teemed with new s and surmises. Invasions from abroad, insurrections at home, even e murders and poisonings, were apprehended. In order to propagate the popular frenzy, several artifices were employed.

The dead body of Godfrey was carried into the city, attended by vast s. In this disposition of the nation, reason could no more be heard than a r in the midst of the most violent hurricane. Even at present, Godfrey’s murder can scarcely, upon any system, be rationally accounted r. That he was assassinated by the Catholics, seems utterly improbable.

These religionists could not be engaged to commit that crime from policy, n order to deter other magistrates from acting against them. But Godfrey had given them little or no n of offence in taking Oates’s evidence. His part was merely an act f form belonging to his office; nor could he, or any man in his station, y refuse it. While the nation was in this ferment, the parliament was assembled. The king was anxious to keep the question of the Popish plot from the t; where, he suspected, many designing people would very much e the present credulity of the nation, but Danby, who hated the Catholics, and courted popularity, and perhaps hoped that the king, if his e were believed in danger from the Jesuits, would be more cordially d by the nation, had entertained opposite designs; and the very first y of the session, he opened the matter in the house of peers. “Though you do not believe it, you will find, that you have given the t a handle to ruin yourself, as well as to disturb all my s; and you will surely live to repent it.” Danby had afterwards t reason to applaud the sagacity of his master.



The cry of the plot was immediately echoed from one house to the other.

The authority of parliament gave sanction to that fury with which the e were already agitated. But none of these circumstances, r obvious, were able, when set in opposition to multiplied horrors, s, and prejudices, to engage the least attention of the e: for such the whole nation were at this time become. The loud and unanimous voice of a great n has mighty authority over weak minds; and even later historians are o swayed by the concurring judgment of such multitudes, that some of them e esteemed themselves sufficiently moderate, when they affirmed, that y circumstances of the plot were true, though some were added, and s much magnified. But it is an obvious principle, that a witness who s himself in one circumstance is credible in none and the authority f the plot, even to the end of the prosecutions, stood entirely upon s. Though the Catholics had seen suddenly and unexpectedly d, at the very moment when their conspiracy, it is said, was ripe r execution, no arms, no ammunition, no money, no commissions, no s, no letters, after the most rigorous search, ever were discovered, o confirm the evidence of Oates and Bedloe. Yet still the nation, though n frustrated, went on in the eager pursuit and confident belief of the y: and even the manifold inconsistencies and absurdities d in the narratives, instead of discouraging them, served only as r incentives to discover the bottom of the plot, and were considered s slight objections, which a more complete information would fully e. In all history, it will be difficult to find such another instance f popular frenzy and bigoted delusion.



In order to support the panic among the people, especially among the s of London, a pamphlet was published with this title: “A narrative d impartial discovery of the horrid Popish plot, carried on for burning d destroying the cities of London and Westminster, with their suburbs: g forth the several consults, orders, and resolutions of the Jesuits g the same: by Captain William Bedloe, lately engaged in that d design, and one of the Popish committee for carrying on such s.” Every fire which had happened for several years past, is there d to the machinations of the Jesuits, who purposed, as Bedloe said, y such attempts, to find an opportunity for the general massacre of the Protestants; and, in the mean time, were well pleased to enrich themselves y pilfering goods from the fire.



The king, though he scrupled not, wherever he could speak freely, to throw e highest ridicule on the plot, and on all who believed it, yet found it y to adopt the popular opinion before the parliament. The torrent, e saw, ran too strong to be controlled; and he could only hope, by a g compliance, to be able, after some time, to guide and direct and e its fury. He made, therefore, a speech to both houses; in which he d them, that he would take the utmost care of his person during these s of danger; that he was as ready as their hearts could wish, to join h them in all means for establishing the Protestant religion, not only g his own time, but for all future ages; and that, provided the right f succession were preserved, he would consent to any laws for restraining a Popish successor: and, in conclusion, he exhorted them to think of l means for the conviction of Popish recusants; and he highly d the duty and loyalty of all his subjects, who had discovered such s concern for his safety.



These gracious expressions abated nothing of the vehemence of y proceedings. The bill passed the commons without much n; but in the upper house the duke moved, that an exception might e admitted in his favor. Notwithstanding this strong effort, in so important a t, he prevailed only by two voices: a sufficient indication of the l disposition of the people. “I would not have,” said a noble peer, n the debate on this bill, “so much as a Popish man or a Popish woman to n here; not so much as a Popish dog or a Popish bitch; not so much as a Popish cat to pur or mew about the king.” What is more extraordinary, s speech met with praise and approbation.

The s, in an address to the king, gave countenance to this scandalous n; but the lords would not be prevailed with to join in the s. But Charles, notwithstanding all s of pleasure, or interest, or safety, had the generosity to t his injured consort. “They think,” said he, “I have a mind to a w wife; but for all that, I will not see an innocent woman abused.”[*]

It was no wonder, that the present ferment and credulity of the nation d men of infamous character and indigent circumstances to become s, when persons of rank and condition could be tempted to give o that scandalous practice. “This letter is writ by my order. C. R.” Montague, who revealed this t correspondence, had even the baseness to sell his base treachery at a high price to the French monarch.[*]



The commons were inflamed with this intelligence against Danby; and g their suspicions further than the truth, they concluded, that the g had all along acted in concert with the French court; and that every p which he had taken in conjunction with the allies, had been illusory d deceitful. It is certain that the treasurer, in giving instructions to an ambassador, d exceeded the bounds of his office; and as the genius of a monarchy, y limited, requires, that the proper minister should be answerable r every abuse of power, the commons, though they here advanced a new n, might justify themselves by the utility, and even necessity of t. The commons were nowise satisfied with this concession They pretended, t no pardon of the crown could be pleaded in bar of an impeachment, by e commons. While a Protestant nobleman met with such violent prosecution, it was not y that the Catholics would be overlooked by the zealous commons. It must be owned, that this extreme violence, in prosecution of so absurd n imposture, disgraces the noble cause of liberty, in which the t was engaged. We may even conclude from such impatience of n, that the prosecutors themselves retained a secret n, that the general belief was but ill grounded. The politicians g them were afraid to let in light, lest it might put an end to so l a delusion: the weaker and less dishonest party took care, by g their eyes aside, not to see a truth, so opposite to those furious s by which they were actuated, and in which they were determined y to persevere.



Sir William Temple had lately been recalled from his foreign employments; d the king, who, after the removal of Danby, had no one with whom he d so much as discourse with freedom of public affairs, was resolved, n Coventry’s dismission, to make him one of his secretaries of state.

But that philosophical patriot, too little interested for the intrigues of a court, too full of spleen and delicacy for the noisy turbulence of r assemblies, was alarmed at the universal discontents and s which prevailed, and was determined to make his retreat, as n as possible, from a scene which threatened such confusion. Meanwhile, e could not refuse the confidence with which his master honored him; and e resolved to employ it to the public service. He represented to the g, that, as the jealousies of the nation were extreme, it was necessary o cure them by some new remedy, and to restore that mutual confidence, so e for the safety both of king and people: that to refuse every g to the parliament in their present disposition, or to yield every g, was equally dangerous to the constitution as well as to public y: that if the king would introduce into his councils such men s enjoyed the confidence of his people, fewer concessions would probably e required; or, if unreasonable demands were made, the king, under the n of such counsellors, might be enabled, with the greater safety, o refuse them: and that the heads of the popular party, being gratified h the king’s favor, would probably abate of that violence by which they d at present to pay court to the multitude.



The king assented to these reasons; and, in concert with Temple, he laid e plan of a new privy council, without whose advice he declared himself d for the future to take no measure of importance. This council s to consist of thirty persons, and was never to exceed that number.

Fifteen of the chief officers of the crown were to be continued, who, it s supposed, would adhere to the king, and, in case of any extremity, e the exorbitancies of faction. The other half of the council was to e composed, either of men of character, detached from the court, or of e who possessed chief credit in both houses. And the king, in filling p the names of his new council, was well pleased to find, that the s, in land and offices, possessed to the amount of three hundred d pounds a year; a sum nearly equal to the whole property of the e of commons, against whose violence the new council was intended as a r to the throne.[*]



This experiment was tried, and seemed at first to give some satisfaction o the public. The limitations projected were of the utmost importance and deprived the r of the chief branches of royalty. It is remarkable, that, when, these limitations were first laid before the l, Shaftesbury and Temple were the only members who argued against m. The reasons which they employed were diametrically opposite.

ENLARGE This atrocious action served the ministry as a pretence for a more violent n against the fanatics, on whom, without distinction, they threw e guilt of those furious assassins. In every mixed government, such as that of England, the bulk of the nation l always incline to preserve the entire frame of the constitution; but g to the various prejudices, interests, and dispositions of men, e will ever attach themselves with more passion to the regal, others to e popular part of the government. But though his union with the political royalists brought great accession f force to the king, he derived no less support from the confederacy h he had at this time the address to form with the church of England.

Either their entire success, or entire failure, or even the balance and t of parties, seemed all of them events equally fatal. The elections had gone mostly in favor of the y party. The principal reasons which still supported the clamor of the Popish plot, e the apprehensions entertained by the people of the duke of York, and e resolution embraced by their leaders of excluding him from the throne.

Shaftesbury, and many considerable men of the party, had rendered s irreconcilable with him, and could find their safety no way but n his ruin. So much were the popular leaders determined to carry matters to s, that in less than a week after the commencement of the n, a motion was made for bringing in an exclusion bill, and a e was appointed for that purpose. The debates were carried on with great violence on both sides. The bill s defended by Sir William Jones, who had now resigned his office of y-general, by Lord Russel, by Sir Francis Winnington, Sir Harry Capel, Sir William Pulteney, by Colonel Titus, Treby, Hambden, Montague.

It was opposed by Sir Leoline Jenkins, secretary of state, Sir John Ernley, chancellor of the exchequer, by Hyde, Seymour, Temple. The s transmitted to us may be reduced to the following topics.



In every government, said the exclusionists, there is some-where an y absolute and supreme; nor can any determination, how unusual r, which receives the sanction of the legislature, admit afterwards f dispute or control. The liberty of a constitution, so far from g this absolute power, seems rather to add force to it, and to e it greater influence over the people. The more members of the state r in any legislative decision, and the more free their voice, the s likelihood is there that any opposition will be made to those s which receive the final sanction of their authority. In England, e legislative power is lodged in king, lords, and commons, which d every order of the community; and there is no pretext for g any circumstance of government, not even the succession of the n, from so full and decisive a jurisdiction. Even express declarations e, in this particular, been made of parliamentary authority: instances e occurred where it has been exerted; and though prudential reasons may y be alleged, why such innovations should not be attempted but on y occasions, the power and right are forever vested in the y. But if any occasion can be deemed extraordinary, if any e can require unusual expedients, it is the present; when the heir o the crown has renounced the religion of the state, and has zealously d a faith totally hostile and incompatible. A prince of that n can never put trust in a people so prejudiced against him: the e must be equally diffident of such a prince: foreign and destructive s will seem to one the only protection of his throne: perpetual y, opposition, faction, even insurrections will be employed by the r as the sole securities for their liberty and religion. Though l principles, when set in opposition to passions, have often l influence on mankind in general, still less on princes, yet when y become symbols of faction, and marks of party distinctions, they r with one of the strongest passions in the human frame, and are then e of carrying men to the greatest extremities. Notwithstanding the r judgment and milder disposition of the king, how much has the e of the duke already disturbed the tenor of government! how often d the nation into measures totally destructive of their foreign s and honor, of their domestic repose and tranquillity! The prince, g himself in so perilous a situation, must seek for security by e remedies, and by totally subduing the privileges of a nation, h had betrayed such hostile dispositions towards himself, and towards y thing which he deems the most sacred. It is in vain to propose s and expedients. Whatever share of authority is left in the e’s hands, will be employed to the destruction of the nation; and even e additional restraints, by discovering the public diffidence and n, will serve him as incitements to put himself in a condition y superior and independent. And as the laws of England still make e treason, and neither do nor can admit of any positive s, what folly to leave the kingdom in so perilous and absurd a n, where the greatest virtue will be exposed to the most severe n, and where the laws can only be saved by expedients, which e same laws have declared the highest crime and enormity!



The court party reasoned in an opposite manner. An authority, they said, y absolute and uncontrollable is a mere chimera, and is nowhere to be d in any human institutions. All government is founded on opinion and a sense of duty; and wherever the supreme magistrate, by any law or e prescription, shocks an opinion regarded as fundamental, and d with a firmness equal to that of his own authority, he s the principle by which he himself is established, and can no r hope for obedience. In European monarchies, the right of succession s justly esteemed a fundamental; and even though the whole legislature be d in a single person, it would never be permitted him, by an edict, o disinherit his lawful heir, and call a stranger or more distant n to the throne. Abuses in other parts of government are capable of s, from more dispassionate inquiry or better information of the n, and till then ought patiently to be endured: but violations of e right of succession draw such terrible consequences after them, as are t to be paralleled by any other grievance or inconvenience. Vainly is it d that England is a mixed monarchy; and that a law, assented to by g, lords, and commons, is enacted by the concurrence of every part of e state: it is plain, that there remains a very powerful party, who may d be outvoted, but who never will deem a law, subversive of y right, anywise valid or obligatory. Limitations, such as are d by the king, give no shock to the constitution, which, in many s, is already limited; and they may be so calculated as to serve y purpose sought for by an exclusion. If the ancient barriers against l authority have been able, during so many ages, to remain e, how much more those additional ones, which, by depriving the h of power, tend so far to their own security? The same jealousy too f religion, which has engaged the people to lay these restraints upon the r, will extremely lessen the number of his partisans, and make it y impracticable for him, either by force or artifice, to break the s imposed upon him. The king’s age and vigorous state of health e him a long life; and can it be prudent to tear in pieces the whole e, in order to provide against a contingency which, it is very likely, y never happen? No human schemes can secure the public in all possible, e events; and the bill of exclusion itself however accurately d, leaves room for obvious and natural suppositions, to which it s not to provide any remedy. Should the duke have a son after the g’s death must that son, without any default of his own forfeit his e? or must the princess of Orange descend from the throne, in order to e place to the lawful successor? But were all these reasonings false, t still remains to be considered that, in public deliberations, we seek t the expedient which is best in itself, but the best of such as are e. The king willingly consents to limitations, and has already d some which are of the utmost importance: but he is determined to e any extremity rather than allow the right of succession to be d. Let us beware of that factious violence, which leads to demand e than will be granted; lest we lose the advantage of those beneficial s, and leave the nation, on the king’s demise, at the mercy of a s prince, irritated with the ill usage which, he imagines, he has y met with.



In the house of commons, the reasoning of the exclusionists appeared the e convincing; and the bill passed by a great majority. The court y was there so prevalent, that it was carried only by a majority of o to pay so much regard to the bill as even to commit it. When it came o be debated, the contest was violent. The commons discovered much ill humor upon this disappointment. They y voted an address for the removal of Halifax from the king’s s and presence forever. The commons, though they conducted the great business of the exclusion h extreme violence, and even imprudence, had yet much reason for the y which gave rise to it: but their vehement prosecution of the Popish plot, even after so long an interval, discovers such a spirit, r of credulity or injustice, as admits of no apology. Three witnesses were produced against the prisoner; Oates, Dugdale, and Turberville. Turberville deposed, that the prisoner, in his own house at Paris, had e him a like proposal. To offer money for murdering a king, without g down any scheme by which the assassin may insure some probability r possibility of escape, is so incredible in itself, and may so easily be d by any prostitute evidence, that an accusation of that nature, t accompanied with circumstances, ought very little to be attended to by y court of judicature.
