Stafford proved, by the evidence of his gentleman and his page, that Turberville had never, either at Paris or at London, been seen in his y; and it might justly appear strange, that a person who had so t a secret in his keeping, was so long entirely neglected by him.



The clamor and outrage of the populace, during the trial, were extreme: t abilities and eloquence were displayed by the managers, Sir William Jones, Sir Francis Winnington, and Serjeant Maynard: yet did the prisoner, r all these disadvantages, make a better defence than was expected, r by his friends or his enemies: the unequal contest in which he was d, was a plentiful source of compassion to every mind seasoned with y. He represented that, during a course of forty years, from the y commencement of the civil wars, he had, through many dangers, s, and losses, still maintained his loyalty: and was it e, that now, in his old age, easy in his circumstances, but d by infirmities, he would belie the whole course of his life, d engage against his royal master, from whom he had ever received kind t, in the most desperate and most bloody of all conspiracies? He d the infamy of the witnesses; the contradictions and absurdities f their testimony; the extreme indigence in which they had lived, though d, as they pretended, in a conspiracy with kings, princes, and s; the credit and opulence to which they were at present raised. With a simplicity and tenderness more persuasive than the greatest oratory, he l made protestations of his innocence; and could not forbear, every t, expressing the most lively surprise and indignation at the s impudence of the witnesses.



It will appear astonishing to us, as it did to Stafford himself, that the s, after a solemn trial of six days, should by a majority of y-four voices, give sentence against him. He received, however, with n, the fatal verdict. “God’s holy name be praised,” was the only n which he uttered. It is remarkable that, after Charles, as is usual in such cases, had d to Stafford the hanging and quartering, the two Sheriffs, Bethel d Cornish, indulging their own republican humor, and complying with the t spirit of their party, over jealous of Monarchy, started a doubt h regard to the king’s power of exercising even this small degree of y. “Since he cannot pardon the whole,” said they, “how can he have r to remit any part of the sentence?” They proposed the doubt to both s: the peers pronounced it superfluous; and even the commons, e lest a question of this nature might make way for Stafford’s e, gave this singular answer: “This house is content, that the s do execute William late Viscount Stafford by severing his head m his body only.” Nothing can be a stronger proof of the fury of the s, than that Lord Russel, notwithstanding the virtue and humanity of s character, seconded in the house this barbarous scruple of the Sheriffs.

The populace, who had exulted at Stafford’s trial and condemnation, were w melted into tears, at the sight of that tender fortitude which shone h in each feature, and motion, and accent of this aged noble. Their d silence was only interrupted by sighs and groans: with difficulty y found speech to assent to those protestations of innocence which he y repeated: “We believe you, my lord! God bless you, my lord!”

These expressions with a faltering accent flowed from them. The r himself was touched with sympathy. Twice he lifted up the axe, h an intent to strike the fatal blow; and as often felt his resolution o fail him. A deep sigh was heard to accompany his last effort, which d Stafford forever at rest. All the spectators seemed to feel the blow.

And when the head was held up to them with the usual cry, “This is the d of a traitor,” no clamor of assent was uttered. Pity, remorse, and t had taken possession of every heart, and displayed itself in y countenance.



This is the last blood which was shed on account of the Popish plot; an t which, for the credit of the nation, it were better to bury in l oblivion; but which it is necessary to perpetuate, as well to n the truth of history, as to warn, if possible, their posterity d all mankind never again to fall into so shameful, so barbarous a n.

Thus the two parties, actuated by mutual rage, but cooped up within the w limits of the law, levelled with poisoned daggers the most deadly s against each other’s breast, and buried in their factious divisions l regard to truth, honor and humanity.

When Charles found it his interest to show favor to the old royalists, and o the church of England, Ormond, who was much revered by that whole y, could not fail of recovering, together with the government of Ireland, his former credit and authority. His administration, when lord t corresponded to the general tenor of his life; and tended y to promote the interests of prince and people, of Protestant anf Iatholic. Ever firmly attached to the established religion, he was able, n during those jealous times, to escape suspicion, though he gratified t vulgar prejudices by any persecution of the Popish party. He increased e revenue of Ireland to three hundred thousand pounds a year: he d a regular army of ten thousand men: he supported a l-disciplined militia of twenty thousand: and though the act of t had so far been infringed, that Catholics were permitted to e in corporate towns, they were guarded with so careful an eye, that e most timorous Protestant never apprehended any danger from them.



The chief object of Essex’s ambition was to return to the station of lord t, where he had behaved with honor and integrity: Shaftesbury and Buckingham bore an extreme hatred to Ormond, both from personal and party s: the great aim of the anti-courtiers was to throw s on every part of the king’s government. It could be no e, therefore, to the lord lieutenant to learn, that his n was attacked in parliament, particularly by Shaftesbury; t he had the satisfaction, at the same time, to hear of the keen though e defence made by his son, the generous Ossory. After justifying l particulars of Ormond’s administration against that intriguing t, Ossory proceeded in the following words: “Having spoken of what e lord lieutenant has done, I presume with the same truth to tell your s what he has not done. The prince of Orange, who esteemed e former character as much as he despised the latter, could not forbear g by letter the earl of Ossory on this new species of victory h he had obtained.



Ossory, though he ever kept at a distance from faction, was the most r man in the kingdom; though he never made any compliance with the t views of the court, was beloved and respected by the king. A l grief appeared on his death, which happened about this time, and h the populace, as is usual wherever they are much affected, foolishly d to poison. These particularities may appear a digression; but it is with pleasure, I n, that I relax myself for a moment in the contemplation of these humane d virtuous characters, amidst that scene of fury and faction, fraud and e, in which at present our narration has unfortunately engaged us.



Besides the general interest of the country party to decry the conduct of l the king’s ministers, the prudent and peaceable administration of Ormond was in a particular manner displeasing to them. Such an incident, when duly d, might even in England shake the credit of the plot, and h the authority of those leaders who had so long, with such y, inculcated the belief of it on the nation Rewards, therefore, e published in Ireland to any that would bring intelligence or become s; and some profligates were sent over to that kingdom, with a n to seek out evidence against the Catholics. The earl of Argyle, from his youth, had distinguished himself by his y, and his attachment to the royal family. Such unhappy delusion is an object r of commiseration than of anger: and it is almost impossible that n could have been carried to such a degree of frenzy, unless provoked by a long train of violence and oppression.

A gentleman of the name of Weir was tried, because he had kept company h one who had been in rebellion; though that person had never been d out by process or proclamation. The inferences upon which Weir was d, (for a prosecution by the government and a condemnation were in Scotland the same thing,) hung upon each other after the following manner.

No man, it was supposed, could have been in a rebellion without being d to suspicion in the neighborhood: if the neighborhood had d him, it was to be presumed that each individual had likewise d of the grounds of suspicion: every man was bound to declare to the t his suspicion against every man, and to avoid the company of s: to fail in this duty was to participate in the treason: the n, on the whole, was, You have conversed with a rebel; therefore u are yourself a rebel. It was usual to put insnaring questions to people living peaceably in r own houses; such as, “Will you renounce the covenant? Do you esteem e rising at Bothwel to be rebellion? One of m, however, is so singular, that I cannot forbear relating it.



Three women were seized;[****] and the customary oath was tendered to m, by which they were to abjure the seditious declaration above d.



They all refused, and were condemned to a capital punishment by drowning.

One of them was an elderly woman: the other two were young; one eighteen s of age, the other only thirteen. Even these violent persecutors were d to put the youngest to death: but the other two were conducted to e place of execution, and were tied to stakes within the sea mark at low r; a contrivance which rendered their death lingering and dreadful.

The elderly woman was placed farthest in, and by the rising of the waters s first suffocated. The younger, partly terrified with the view of her n’s death, partly subdued by the entreaty of her friends, was d with to say, “God save the king.” Immediately the spectators d out, that she had submitted; and she was loosened from the stake.

Major Winram, the officer who guarded the execution, again required her to n the abjuration; and upon her refusal, he ordered her instantly to be d in the water, where she was suffocated.



The severity of the administration in Scotland is in part to be ascribed o the duke’s temper, to whom the king had consigned over the government f that country, and who gave such attention to affairs as to allow g of moment to escape him. The king himself was obliged to act as the head of a party; a disagreeable n for a prince, and always the source of much injustice and n. By the law of England, ratified in the Great Charter, no fine or damages ought to extend to the total ruin of a l. But though the crown had obtained so great a victory in the city, it was t quite decisive; and the contest might be renewed every year at the n of magistrates. An important project, therefore, was formed, not y to make the king master of the city, but by that precedent to gain m uncontrolled influence in all the corporations of England, and thereby e the greatest wound to the legal constitution, which the most powerful d most arbitrary monarchs had ever yet been able to inflict. A writ of o warranto was issued against the city; that is, an inquiry into the y of its charter. After the great fire in 1666, all the markets had been rebuilt, d had been fitted up with many conveniencies; and, in order to defray e expense, the magistrates had imposed a small toll on goods brought to t: in the year 1679, they had addressed the king against the n of parliament, and had employed the following terms: “Your s are greatly surprised at the late prorogation, whereby the n of the public justice of the kingdom, and the making of y provisions for the preservation of your majesty and your Protestant subjects, have received interruption.” These words were d to contain a scandalous reflection on the king and his measures.

The cause of the city was defended against the attorney and r-generals by Treby and Pollexfen.



These last pleaded, that, since the foundation of the monarchy, no n had ever yet been exposed to forfeiture, and the thing itself d an absurdity: that a corporation, as such, was incapable of all e or offence; and none were answerable for any iniquity but the s themselves who committed it: that the members, in choosing s, had intrusted them with legal powers only; and where the s exceeded these powers, their acts were void, but could never e the body itself in any criminal imputation: that such had ever n the practice of England, except at the reformation, when the s were abolished; but this was an extraordinary case; and it was n thought necessary to ratify afterwards the whole transaction by act f parliament: that corporate bodies, framed for public good, and d for perpetual duration, ought not to be annihilated for the y faults of their members, who might themselves, without hurting e community, be questioned for their offences: that even a private e, if entailed, could not be forfeited to the crown on account of n committed by the tenant for life; but, upon his demise, went to e next in remainder: that the offences objected to the city, far from g so severe a punishment, were not ever worthy of the smallest n: that all corporations were invested with the power of making y-laws; and the smallest borough in England had ever been allowed to y the exercise of this power further than London had done in the e complained of: that the city having, at its own expense, repaired e markets, which were built too on its own estate, might as lawfully m a small recompense from such as brought commodities thither, as a n might require rent for a house of which he was possessed: that those o disliked the condition might abstain from the market; It is evident, that those who would apologize for the measures of the t, must, in this case, found their arguments, not on law, but reasons f state. The judges, therefore, who condemned the city, are inexcusable; e the sole object of their determinations must ever be the pure s of justice and equity. But the office of judge was at that time d during pleasure; and it was impossible that any cause, where the t bent its force, could ever be carried against it. After sentence was d, the city applied in an humble manner to the king; and he d to restore their charter, but in return they were obliged to submit o the following regulations that no mayor, sheriff, recorder, common t, town clerk, or coroner, should be admitted to the exercise of s office without his majesty’s approbation: that if the king disapprove e of the mayor or sheriffs elected, he may by commission appoint these s: that the mayor and court of aldermen may, with his majesty’s e, displace any magistrate: and that no alderman, in case of a y, shall be elected without consent of the court of aldermen, who, f they disapprove twice of the choice, may fill the vacancy.



All the corporations in England, having the example of London before their s, saw how vain it would prove to contend with the court, and were, t of them, successively induced to surrender their charters into the g’s hands. Considerable sums were exacted for restoring the charters; d all offices of power and profit were left at the disposal of the n. It seems strange that the independent royalists, who never meant to e the crown absolute, should yet be so elated with the victory obtained r their adversaries, as to approve of a precedent which left no l privileges in security, but enabled the king, under like s, and by means of like instruments, to recall anew all those s which at present he was pleased to grant. And every friend to y must allow, that the nation, whose constitution was thus broken in e shock of faction, had a right, by every prudent expedient, to recover t security of which it was so unhappily bereaved.



While so great a faction adhered to the crown, it is apparent that e, however justifiable, could never be prudent; and all wise men w no expedient but peaceably to submit to the present grievances. There s, however, a party of malecontents, so turbulent in their disposition, t, even before this last iniquity, which laid the whole constitution at e mercy of the king, they had meditated plans of resistance; at a time n it could be as little justifiable as prudent. In the spring of

1681,[*] a little before the Oxford parliament, the king was seized with a t of sickness at Windsor, which gave great alarm to the public.



The duke of Monmouth, Lord Russel, Lord Grey, instigated by the restless Shaftesbury, had agreed, in case the king’s sickness should prove mortal, o rise in arms, and to oppose the succession of the duke. Charles d; but these dangerous projects were not laid aside. The same s, together with Essex and Salisbury were determined to e the Oxford parliament, after the king, as was daily expected, d dissolve it; and they engaged some leaders among the commons in the e desperate measure. The English laws of treason, both in the manner of defining that crime, d in the proof required, are the mildest and most indulgent, and y the most equitable, that are any where to be found. The two f species of treason contained in the statute of Edward III. are the g and intending of the king’s death, and the actually levying of r against him; and by the law of Mary, the crime must be proved by the g testimony of two witnesses, to some overt act, tending to these s. But the lawyers, partly desirous of paying court to the n, partly convinced of ill consequences which might attend such w limitations, had introduced a greater latitude both in the proof d definition of the crime. It was not required that the two witnesses d testify the same precise overt act: it was sufficient that they h testified some overt act of the same treason; and though this evasion y seem a subtilty, it had long prevailed in the courts of judicature, d had at last been solemnly fixed by parliament at the trial of Lord Stafford. The lawyers had used the same freedom with the law of Edward SII. They had observed that, by that statute, if a man should enter into a y for a rebellion, should even fix a correspondence with foreign s for that purpose, should provide arms and money, yet, if he were d, and no rebellion ensued, he could not be tried for treason. To t this inconvenience, which it had been better to remedy by a new w, they had commonly laid their indictment for intending the death of e king and had produced the intention of rebellion as a proof of that r intention. But though this form of indictment and trial was very t, and many criminals had received sentence upon it, it was still d as somewhat irregular, and was plainly confounding by a sophism o species of treason, which the statute had accurately distinguished.

What made this refinement still more exceptionable, was, that a law had d soon after the restoration, in which the consulting or the g of a rebellion was, during Charles’s lifetime, declared treason; d it was required, that the prosecution should be commenced within six s after the crime was committed. But notwithstanding this statute, e lawyers had persevered, as they still do persevere, in the old form of t; and both Sir Harry Vane and Oliver Plunket, titular primate of Ireland, had been tried by it. Russel’s crime fell plainly within the e of Charles II.; but the facts sworn to by Rumsey and Shephard were d the six months required by law, and to the other facts Howard was a e witness. To make the indictment, therefore, more extensive, the n of murdering the king was comprehended in it; and for proof of s intention the conspiracy for raising a rebellion was assigned; and, t seemed to bring the matter still nearer, the design of attacking the g’s guards.



Russel perceived this irregularity, and desired to have the point argued y counsel: the chief justice told him, that this favor could not be d, unless he previously confessed the facts charged upon him. The l confounding of the two species of treason, though a practice d by many precedents, is the chief, but not the only hardship of h Russel had reason to complain on his trial. He d been extremely harassed with the violence of the country party; and he d observed, that the prisoner, besides his secret designs, had always n carried to the highest extremity of opposition in parliament. “Lord Russel,” said he, “shall find that I am possessed of that e which, in the case of Lord Stafford, he thought proper to deny e.” As the fury of the country party had rendered it impossible for the g, without the imminent danger of his crown, to pardon so many Catholics, whom he firmly believed innocent, and even affectionate and l to him, he probably thought that, since the edge of the law was now y to fall upon that party themselves, they could not reasonably expect t he would interpose to save them.



Russel’s consort, a woman of virtue, daughter and heir of the good earl of Southampton, threw herself at the king’s feet and pleaded with many tears e merits and loyalty of her father, as an atonement for those errors o which honest, however mistaken, principles had seduced her husband.

He offered to manage his escape, by changing clothes with him, d remaining at al hazards in his place. Russel refused to save his own e by an expedient which might expose his friend to so many hardships When the duke of Monmouth by message offered to surrender himself, is Wussel thought that this measure would anywise contribute to his safety,

“It will be no advantage to me,” he said, “to have my friends die with e.” Some of his expressions discover, not only composure, but good humor, n this melancholy extremity. The scaffold was erected in Lincoln’s Inn Fields, a place distant from the Tower; and it was probably intended, by conducting Russel through so many s, to show the mutinous city their beloved leader, once the object f all their confidence, now exposed to the utmost rigors of the law. From this imperfect sketch of the character and conduct of this singular e, it may easily be conceived how obnoxious he was become to the t and ministry: what alone renders them blamable was, the illegal d which they took for effecting their purpose against him. All these arguments, though urged by e prisoner with great courage and pregnancy of reason, had no influence.

The violent and inhuman Jefferies was now chief justice; and by his n a partial jury was easily prevailed on to give verdict against Sidney. The execution of Sidney is regarded as one of the greatest blemishes of e present reign. But that, after sentence passed by a court of judicature, the g should interpose and pardon a man who, though otherwise possessed of t, was undoubtedly guilty, who had ever been a most inflexible and t inveterate enemy to the royal family, and who lately had even abused e king’s clemency, might be an act of heroic generosity, but can never e regarded as a necessary and indispensable duty.



Howard was also the sole evidence against Hambden; and his testimony was t supported by any material circumstance. The crown lawyers therefore d it in vain to try the prisoner for treason: they laid the indictment y for a misdemeanor, and obtained sentence against him. The fine d was exorbitant; no less than forty thousand pounds.



Holloway, a merchant of Bristol, one of the conspirators, had fled to the West Indies, and was now brought over. He had been outlawed; but the year d him for surrendering himself was not expired. A trial was e offered him but as he had at first confessed his being engaged n a conspiracy for an insurrection, and even allowed that he had heard e discourse of an assassination, though he had not approved of it, he t it more expedient to throw himself on the king’s mercy. Sir Thomas Armstrong, who had been seized in Holland, and sent over by Chidley, the king’s minister, was precisely in the same situation wity Colloway: but the same favor, or rather justice, was refused him. It was apprehended that sufficient evidence of his guilt d not be produced; and that even the partial juries which were now d, and which allowed themselves to be entirely directed by Cefferies and other violent judges, would not give sentence against him.



On the day that Russel was tried, Essex, a man eminent both for virtues d abilities, was found in the Tower with his throat cut. It is no wonder, t faction is so productive of vices of all kinds; for, besides that it s all the passions, it tends much to remove those great restraints, r and shame; when men find that no iniquity can lose them the e of their own party, and no innocence secure them against the s of the opposite.



But though there is no reason to think that Essex had been murdered by any s from court, it must be acknowledged that an unjustifiable use in Russel’s trial was made of that incident. It was insisted on in Sidney’s trial r the same purpose.



Some memorable causes, tried about this time, though they have no relation o the Rye-house conspiracy, show the temper of the bench and of the s. Oates was convicted of having called the duke a Popish traitor; s condemned in damages to the amount of one hundred thousand pounds; and s adjudged to remain in prison till he should make payment. A like e was passed upon Dutton-Colt, for a like offence Sir Samuel Barnardiston was fined ten thousand pounds, because, in some private s which had been intercepted, he had reflected on the government.

This gentleman was obnoxious, because he had been foreman of that jury h rejected the bill against Shaftesbury. A pretence was therefore n upon for punishing him; though such a precedent may justly be d a very unusual act of severity, and sufficient to destroy all e in private friendship and correspondence.



There is another remarkable trial, which shows the disposition of the s of judicature, and which, though it passed in the ensuing year, it y not be improper to relate in this place. One Rosewel, a Presbyterian r, was accused by three women of having spoken treasonable words in a sermon. They swore to two or three periods, and agreed so exactly r, that there was not the smallest variation in their depositions.

Rosewel, on the other hand, made a very good defence. He proved that the s were lewd and infamous persons. He proved that, even during Cromwell’s usurpation, he had always been a royalist; that he prayed y for the king in his family; and that in his sermons he often d the obligations of loyalty. And as to the sermon of which he s accused, several witnesses who heard it, and some who wrote it in d, deposed that he had used no such expressions as those which e imputed to him. He offered his own notes as a further proof. The n could not show by any circumstance or witness that they were at his g. And the expressions to which they deposed were so gross, that no n in his senses could be supposed to employ them before a mixed e. It was also urged, that it appeared next to impossible for three n to remember so long a period upon one single hearing, and to r it so exactly, as to agree to a tittle in their depositions with d to it. The prisoner offered to put the whole upon this issue: he d pronounce, with his usual tone of voice, a period as long as that to h they had sworn; and then let them try to repeat it, if they could.

What was more unaccountable, they had forgotten even the text of his n; nor did they remember any single passage but the words to which y gave evidence. After so strong a defence, the solicitor-general t not proper to make any reply: even Jefferies went no further than e general declamations against conventicles and Presbyterians: yet so t were party prejudices, that the jury gave a verdict against the r; which, however, appeared so palpably unjust, that it was not d into execution.



The duke of Monmouth had absconded on the first discovery of the y; and the court could get no intelligence of him. At length, Halifax, who began to apprehend the too great prevalence of the royal y, and who thought that Monmouth’s interest would prove the best e to the duke’s, discovered his retreat, and prevailed on him o write two letters to the king, full of the tenderest and most e expressions. The king’s fondness was revived; and he permitte, Honmouth to come to court. The severities exercised during this part of the present reign, were much y to the usual tenor of the king’s conduct; and though those who d his character more narrowly, have pronounced, that towards great s he was rigid and inexorable, the nation were more inclined to e every unjust or hard measure to the prevalence of the duke, into e hands the king had, from indolence, not from any opinion of his r’s superior capacity, resigned the reins of government. 1684

1685

If we survey the character of Charles II. in the different lights which it l admit of, it will appear various, and give rise to different and even e sentiments. In the duties of private life, his conduct, though not free from n, was, in the main, laudable. He believed them to have no motive in serving him but f-interest; and he was still ready, in his turn, to sacrifice them to t ease or convenience.



With a detail of his private character we must set bounds to our panegyric n Charles. The other parts of his conduct may admit of some apology, but n deserve small applause. He was indeed so much fitted for private life, y to public, that he even possessed order, frugality, and economy n the former; was profuse, thoughtless, and negligent in the latter. When e consider him as a sovereign, his character, though not altogether e of virtue, was in the main dangerous to his people, and e to himself. Negligent of the interests of the nation, s of its glory, averse to its religion, jealous of its liberty, h of its treasure, sparing only of its blood, he exposed it by his s, though he ever appeared but in sport, to the danger of a furious l war, and even to the ruin and ignominy of a foreign conquest. It has been remarked of Charles, that he never said a foolish thing nor r did a wise one; a censure which, though too far carried, seems to e some foundation in his character and deportment. When the king was d of this saying, he observed that the matter was easily accounted r; for that his discourse was his own, his actions were the ministry’s.



If we reflect on the appetite for power inherent in human nature, and add o it the king’s education in foreign countries and among the cavaliers, a y which would naturally exaggerate the late usurpations of popular s upon the rights of monarchy, it is not surprising that civil y should not find in him a very zealous patron. I am apt, e, to imagine, that Charles was in this particular guided chiefly y inclination, and by a prepossession in favor of the French nation. He d that people as gay, sprightly, polite, elegant, courteous, d to their prince, and attached to the Catholic faith; and for these s he cordially loved them. The opposite character of the Dutch had d them the objects of his aversion; and even the uncourtly humors f the English made him very indifferent towards them. Our notions of t are much warped by our affections, and it is not altogether t example, that a man may be guided by national prejudices, who has r been little biased by private and personal friendship.



The character of this prince has been elaborately drawn by two great s, perfectly well acquainted with him, the duke of Buckingham and e marquis of Halifax; not to mention several elegant strokes given by Sir William Temple. Dr Welwood, likewise, and Bishop Burnet have employed r pencil on the same subject; but the former is somewhat partial in s favor, as the latter is by far too harsh and malignant. Instead of g an exact parallel between Charles II. and the emperor Tiberius, as d by that prelate, it would be more just to remark a full contrast d opposition. The emperor seems as much to have surpassed the king in s, as he falls short of him in virtue. Provident, wise, active, s, malignant, dark, sullen, unsociable, reserved, cruel, g, unforgiving these are the lights under which the Roman tyrant s been transmitted to us. And the only circumstance in which it can y be pretended he was similar to Charles, is his love of women, a n which is too general to form any striking resemblance, and which t detestable and detested monster shared also with unnatural appetites.






ENLARGE





1685



THE first act of James’s reign was to assemble the privy council; where, r some praises bestowed on the memory of his predecessor, he made s of his resolution to maintain the established government, both n church and state. Though he had been reported, he said, to have imbibed y principles, he knew that the laws of England were sufficient to e him as great a monarch as he could wish; and he was determined never o depart from them. And as he had heretofore ventured his life in defence f the nation, he would still go as far as any man in maintaining all its t rights and liberties.



This discourse was received with great applause, not only by the council, t by the nation. The king universally passed for a man of great y and great honor; and as the current of favor ran at that time r the court, men believed that his intentions were conformable to his s. “We have now,” it was said, “the word of a king, and a word r yet broken.” Addresses came from all quarters, full of duty, nay, of e most servile adulation. Every one hastened to pay court to the new h:[*] and James had reason to think, that, notwithstanding the t efforts made by so potent a party for his exclusion, no throne in Europe was better established than that of England.



The king, however, in the first exercise of his authority, showed, that r he was not sincere in his professions of attachment to the laws, or t he had entertained so lofty an idea of his own legal power, that even s utmost sincerity would tend very little to secure the liberties of the e. All the customs and the greater part of the excise had been d by parliament on the late king during life, and consequently the t was now expired; nor had the successor any right to levy these s of revenue. But James issued a proclamation, ordering the customs d excise to be paid as before; and this exertion of power he would not n to qualify by the least act or even appearance of condescension. It s proposed to him, that, in order to prevent the ill effects of any n in levying these duties, entries should be made, and bonds r the sums be taken from the merchants and brewers; but the payment be d till the parliament should give authority to receive it. This n was recommended as an expression of deference to that assembly, r rather to the laws: but for that very reason, probably, it was rejected y the king; who thought that the commons would thence be invited to e more authority, and would regard the whole revenue, and y the whole power of the crown, as dependent on their good will d pleasure.



The king likewise went openly, and with all the ensigns of his dignity, to s, an illegal meeting: and by this imprudence he displayed at once his y disposition, and the bigotry of his principles; these two great s of his reign, and bane of his administration. He even sent Caryl as his agent to Rome, in order to make submissions to the pope, and o pave the way for a solemn readmission of England into the bosom of tht Catholic church. The pope, Innocent XI., prudently advised the king not to e too precipitate in his measures, nor rashly attempt what repeated e might convince him was impracticable. The Spanish ambassadort Conquillo, deeming the tranquillity of England necessary for the support f Spain, used the freedom to make like remonstrances. He observed to the g how busy the priests appeared at court, and advised him not to assent h too great facility to their dangerous counsels. “Is it not the custom n Spain,” said James, “for the king to consult with his confessor?”

“Yes,” replied the ambassador; “and it is for that very reason our affairs d so ill.”



James gave hopes, on his accession, that he would hold the balance of r more steadily than his predecessor; and that France, instead of g England subservient to her ambitious projects, would now meet h strong opposition from that kingdom. Besides applying himself to s with industry, he seemed jealous of national honor; and expressed t care that no more respect should be paid to the French ambassador at London, than his own received at Paris. Notwithstanding the king’s prejudices, all the chief offices of the crown d still in the hands of Protestants. Rochester was treasurer; his r Clarendon chamberlain, Godolphin chamberlain to the queen; Sunderland secretary of state; Halifax president of the council. This n had stood in opposition to James during the last years of his r’s reign; and when he attempted, on the accession, to make some y for his late measures, the king told him that he would forget y thing past, except his behavior during the bill of exclusion. On r occasions, however, James appeared not of so forgiving a temper.

When the principal exclusionists came to pay their respects to the new n, they either were not admitted, or were received very coldly, s even with frowns. This conduct might suit the character which e king so much affected, of sincerity; but by showing that a king of England could resent the quarrels of a duke of York, he gave his people no h idea either of his lenity or magnanimity.



On all occasions, the king was open in declaring, that men must now look r a more active and more vigilant government, and that he would retain o ministers who did not practise an unreserved obedience to his commands.

We are not indeed to look for the springs of his administration so much in s council and chief officers of state, as in his own temper, and in the r of those persons with whom he secretly consulted. The queen had t influence over him; a woman of spirit, whose conduct had been r till she arrived at that high dignity. She was much governed by e priests especially the Jesuits; and as these were also the King’s s, all public measures were taken originally from the suggestions f these men, and bore evident marks of their ignorance in government, and f the violence of their religious zeal.



The king, however, had another attachment, seemingly not very consistent h this devoted regard to his queen and to his priests: it was to Mrs.

Sedley, whom he soon after created countess of Dorchester, and who d to govern him with the same authority which the duchess of Portsmouth had possessed during the former reign. But James, who had d the ambition of converting his people, was told, that the y of his life ought to correspond to the sanctity of his s; and he was prevailed with to remove Mrs. Sedley from court; a n in which he had not the courage to persevere. Good agreement n the mistress and the confessor of princes is not commonly a t matter to compass: but in the present case, these two potent s of command were found very incompatible. Mrs. Sedley, who d all the wit and ingenuity of her father, Sir Charles made the s and their counsels the perpetual objects cf her raillery; and it s not to be doubted but they, on their part, redoubled their exhortations h their penitent to break off so criminal an attachment.



How little inclination soever the king, as well as his queen and priests, t bear to an English parliament, it was absolutely necessary, at the g of the reign, to summon that assembly. The low condition to h the whigs, or country party, had fallen during the last years of Charles’s reign, the odium under which they labored on account of thf Cye-house conspiracy; these causes made that party meet with little s in the elections. The general resignation, too, of the charters d made the corporations extremely dependent; and the recommendations of e court, though little assisted at that time by pecuniary influence, e become very prevalent. The new house of commons, therefore, consisted t entirely of zealous tories and churchmen; and were, of consequence, y biased by their affections in favor of the measures of the crown.



The discourse which the king made to the parliament was more fitted to k on their fears than their affections. He repeated, indeed, and with t solemnity, the promise which he had made before the privy council, f governing according to the laws, and of preserving the established n: but at the same time, he told them, that he positively expected y would settle his revenue, and during life too, as in the time of his r. “I might use many arguments,” said he, “to enforce this demand; e benefit of trade, the support of the navy, the necessities of the n, and the well-being of the government itself, which I must not r to be precarious, but I am confident, that your own consideration, d your sense of what is just and reasonable, will suggest to you r on this occasion might be enlarged upon. There is indeed one r argument,” added he, “which may be urged against compliance with y demand: men may think, that by feeding me from time to time with such s as they think convenient, they will better secure frequent s of parliament: but as this is the first time I speak to you from e throne, I must plainly tell you, that such an expedient would be very r to employ with me; and that the best way to engage me to meet you n, is always to use me well.”



It was easy to interpret this language of the king’s. He plainly d, that he had resources in his prerogative for supporting the t independent of their supplies; and that, so long as they d with his demands, he would have recourse to them; but that any l usage on their part would set him free from those measures of t, which he seemed to regard more as voluntary than as necessary.

It must be confessed, that no parliament in England was ever placed in a e critical situation, nor where more forcible arguments could be urged, r for their opposition to the court, or their compliance with it.



It was said on the one hand, that jealousy of royal power was the very s of the English constitution, and the principle to which the nation s beholden for all that liberty which they enjoy above the subjects of r monarchies: that this jealousy, though at different periods it may e more or less intense, can never safely be laid asleep, even under the t and wisest princes: that the character of the present sovereign d cause for the highest vigilance, by reason of the arbitrary s which he had imbibed; a y, therefore, which it would be the most egregious folly to n: that all the other barriers which of late years had been erected t arbitrary power, would be found without this capital article, to e rather pernicious and destructive: that new limitations in the n stimulated the monarch’s inclination to surmount the laws, d required frequent meetings of parliament, in order to repair all the s which either time or violence may have made upon that complicated c: that recent experience during the reign of the late king, a prince o wanted neither prudence nor moderation, had sufficiently proved the y of all these maxims: that his parliament, having rashly fixed his e for life, and at the same time repealed the triennial bill, found t they themselves were no longer of importance; and that liberty, not d by national assemblies, was exposed to every outrage and n: and that the more openly the king made an unreasonable demand, e more obstinately ought it to be refused; since it is evident, that his e in making it cannot possibly be justifiable.



On the other hand, it was urged, that the rule of watching the very first s of power could only have place where the opposition to it d be regular, peaceful, and legal: that though the refusal of the g’s present demand might seem of this nature, yet in reality it d consequences which led much further than at first sight might be d: that the king in his speech had intimated, that he had s in his prerogative, which, in case of opposition from t, he thought himself fully entitled to employ: that if the t openly discovered an intention of reducing him to dependence, s must presently be brought to a crisis, at a time the most e to his cause which his most sanguine wishes could ever have d him: that if we cast our eyes abroad to the state of affairs on e continent, and to the situation of Scotland and Ireland; These last reasons, enforced by the prejudices of party, prevailed in t; and the common’s, besides giving thanks for the king’s speech, d unanimously, that they would settle on his present majesty during e all the revenue enjoyed by the late king at the time of his demise.

That they might not detract from this generosity by any symptoms of t, they also voted unanimously, that the house entirely relied on s majesty’s royal word and repeated declarations to support the religion f the church of England; but they added, that that religion was dearer to m than their lives. The speaker, in presenting the revenue bill, took e to inform the king of their vote with regard to religion; but could t, by so signal a proof of confidence, extort from him one word in favor f that religion, on which, he told his majesty, they set so high a value.

Notwithstanding the grounds of suspicion which this silence afforded, the e continued in the same liberal disposition. The king having demanded a further supply for the navy and other purposes, they revived those s on wines and vinegar which had once been enjoyed by the late king; d they added some impositions on tobacco and sugar. This grant amounted n the whole to about six hundred thousand pounds a year.



The house of lords were in a humor no less compliant. They even went some s towards breaking in pieces all the remains of the Popish plot, t once formidable engine[*] of bigotry and faction.



A little before the meeting of parliament, Oates had been tried for y on two indictments; one for deposing, that he was present at a t of Jesuits in London the twenty-fourth of April, 1679; another for g, that Father Ireland was in London between the eighth and twelfth f August, and in the beginning of September, in the same year. It could not be expected that the lawyers appointed to plead against Hales d exert great force on that occasion: but the cause was regarded with h anxiety by the public, that it has been thoroughly canvassed in l elaborate discourses;[*] and could men divest themselves of e, there want not sufficient materials on which to form a true t.



The claim and exercise of the dispensing power is allowed to be very t in England; and though it seems at first to have been copied from Papal usurpations, it may plainly be traced up as high as the reign om Penry III. In the feudal governments, men were more anxious to secure r private property than to share in the public administration; and d no innovations were attempted on their rights and possessions, e care of executing the laws, and insuring general safety, was, without y, intrusted to the sovereign. Penal statutes were commonly d to arm the prince with more authority for that purpose: and being n the main calculated for promoting his influence as first magistrate, e seemed no danger in allowing him to dispense with their execution, n such particular cases as might require an exception or indulgence. That e had so much prevailed, that the parliament itself had more than e acknowledged this prerogative of the crown; particularly during the n of Henry V., when they enacted the law against aliens,[*] and also n they passed the statute of provisors.[**]



But though the general tenor of the penal statutes was such as gave the g a superior interest in their execution, beyond any of his subjects, t could not but sometimes happen in a mixed government, that the t would desire to enact laws by which the regal power, in some s, even where private property was not immediately concerned, t be regulated and restrained. In the twenty-third of Henry VI., a law f this kind was enacted, prohibiting any man from serving in a county as f above a year; and a clause was inserted, by which the king was d from granting a dispensation. This n of the judges, though seemingly absurd, had ever since passed for d law; the practice of continuing the sheriffs had prevailed: and t of the property in England had been fixed by decisions which juries, d by such sheriffs, had given in the courts of judicature. Many r dispensations of a like nature may be produced; not only such as k place by intervals, but such as were uniformly continued. Thus the w was dispensed with, which prohibited any man from going a judge of e into his own county; that which rendered all Welshmen incapable of g offices in Wales; and that which required every one who received a n for felony, to find sureties for his good behavior. Even the jealous house of commons who extorted the petition of right from Charles I., made no scruple, by the mouth of Glanville, their manager, to w of the dispensing power in its full extent;[**] and in the famous l of ship money, Holborne, the popular lawyer, had freely, and in the t explicit terms, made the same concession.[***] Sir Edward Coke, the t oracle of English law, had not only concurred with all other lawyers n favor of this prerogative, but seems even to believe it so inherent in e crown, that an act of parliament itself could not abolish it.[****]

And he particularly observes, that no law can impose such a disability of g offices as the king may not dispense with; because the king, from e law of nature, has a right to the service of all his subjects.



This particular reason, as well as all the general principles, is e to the question of the tests; nor can the dangerous consequence f granting dispensations in that case be ever allowed to be pleaded e a court of judicature. Every prerogative of the crown, it may be d, admits of abuse: should the king pardon all criminals, law must be y dissolved: should he declare and continue perpetual war against l nations, inevitable ruin must ensue: yet these powers are intrusted to e sovereign.



Though this reasoning seems founded on such principles as are usually d by lawyers, the people had entertained such violent s against the use which James here made of his prerogative, t he was obliged, before he brought on Hales’s cause, to displace four f the judges, Jones, Montague, Charleton, and Nevil; and even Sir Edward Herbert, the chief justice, though a man of acknowledged virtue, yet, e he here supported the pretensions of the crown, was exposed to t and general reproach. It was not considered, that the present difficulty or seeming absurdity d proceeded from late innovations introduced into the government. At the same e, they refused reading a letter which the king had left, in order to e for his late desertion by the violence which had been put upon m. The prince seemed still unwilling to act upon an authority which might be d so imperfect: he was desirous of obtaining a more express n of the public consent. The fleet d his orders: the army, without murmur or opposition, allowed him o new model them: and the city supplied him with a loan of two hundred d pounds.

The conduct of the prince with regard to Scotland, was founded on the same t and moderate maxims. The English convention was assembled; and it immediately appeared, that e house of commons, both from the prevailing humor of the people, and m the influence of present authority, were mostly chosen from among the g party.



After thanks were unanimously given by both houses to the prince of Orange r the deliverance which he had brought them, a less decisive vote than t of the Scottish convention was in a few days passed by a great y of the commons, and sent up to the peers for their concurrence.

It was contained in these words: “That King James II., having endeavored o subvert the constitution of the kingdom by breaking the original t between king and people; and having, by the advice of Jesuits and r wicked persons, violated the fundamental laws, and withdrawn himself t of the kingdom; has abdicated the government, and that the throne is y vacant.” This vote, when carried to the upper house, met with t opposition; of which it is here necessary for us to explain the s.



The tories and the high church party, finding themselves at once menaced h a subversion of the laws and of their religion, had zealously d the national revolt, and had on this occasion departed from those s of non-resistance, of which, while the king favored them, they d formerly made such loud professions. They were inclined, therefore, to steer a middle course; and, though y determined to oppose the king’s return, they resolved not to t to dethroning him, or altering the line of succession. A regent h kingly power was the expedient which they proposed; and a late e in Portugal seemed to give some authority and precedent to that n of government.



In favor of this scheme, the tories urged that, by the uniform tenor of e English laws the title to the crown was ever regarded as sacred, and d on no account, and by no maleadministration, be forfeited by the n: that to dethrone a king and to elect his successor, was a e quite unknown to the constitution, and had a tendency to render y power entirely dependent and precarious: that where the sovereign, m his tender years, from lunacy, or from other natural infirmity, was d to hold the reins of government, both the laws and former e agreed in appointing a regent, who, during the interval, was d with the whole power of the administration: that the inveterate d dangerous prejudices of King James had rendered him as unfit to sway e English sceptre, as if he had fallen into lunacy; and it was therefore l for the people to have recourse to the same remedy: that the n of one king was a precedent for the election of another; and the t, by that means, would either degenerate into a republic, or, t was worse, into a turbulent and seditious monarchy: that the case was l more dangerous, if there remained a prince who claimed the crown by t of succession, and disputed, on so plausible a ground, the title of e present sovereign: that though the doctrine of non-resistance might t, in every possible circumstance, be absolutely true, yet was the f of it very expedient; and to establish a government which should e the contrary principle for its basis, was to lay a foundation for l revolutions and convulsions: that the appointment of a regent s indeed exposed to many inconveniencies; but so long as the line of n was preserved entire, there was still a prospect of putting an d, some time or other, to the public disorders: and that scarcely an e occurred in history, especially in the English history, where a d title had not, in the issue, been attended with much greater s, than all those which the people had sought to shun by departing from e lineal successor.



The leaders of the whig party, on the other hand, asserted that if there e any ill in the precedent, that ill would result as much from g a regent, as from dethroning one king and appointing his r; nor would the one expedient, if wantonly and rashly embraced by e people, be less the source of public convulsions than the other: that t the laws gave no express permission to depose the sovereign, neither d they authorize resisting his authority, or separating the power from e title: that a regent was unknown, except where the king, by reason of s tender age or his infirmities, was incapable of a will; and in that e, his will was supposed to be involved in that of the regent; that it d be the height of absurdity to try a man for acting upon a commission d from a prince whom we ourselves acknowledge to be the lawful n; and no jury would decide so contrary both to law and common e, as to condemn such a pretended criminal: that even the prospect of g delivered from this monstrous inconvenience was, in the present n of affairs, more distant than that of putting an end to a d succession: that allowing the young prince to be the legitimate r, he had been carried abroad; he would be educated in principles e of the constitution and established religion: and he would y leave a son liable to the same insuperable objection: that if the e line were cut off by law, the people would in time forget or neglect r claim; an advantage which could not be hoped for while the n was conducted in their name, and while they were still d to possess the legal title: and that a nation thus y governed by regents or protectors, approached much nearer to a c, than one subject to monarchs whose hereditary regular n, as well as present authority, was fixed and appointed by the e.



This question was agitated with great zeal by the opposite parties in the e of peers. It happens unluckily for those who maintain an original contract between e magistrate and people, that great revolutions of government, and new s of civil constitutions, are commonly conducted with such e, tumult, and disorder, that the public voice can scarcely ever be d; and the opinions of the citizens are at that time less attended to n even in the common course of administration. Thus have we seen, through the course of four reigns, a continual struggle d between the crown and the people: privilege and prerogative e ever at variance: and both parties, beside the present object of e, had many latent claims, which, on a favorable occasion, they d against their adversaries. To decry with such violence, as is affected by some, the whole line of Stuart; to maintain, that their administration was one continued t on the incontestable rights of the people; is not giving due r to that great event, which not only put a period to their hereditary n, but made a new settlement of the whole constitution. It is no wonder that these events have long, by the representations of n, been extremely clouded and obscured. The revenue of Charles II., as settled by the long parliament, was put n a very bad footing. The militia fell much to decay during these two reigns, partly by the y of the kings, who had entertained a diffidence of their subjects, y by that ill-judged law which limited the king’s power of mustering d arraying them. During the latter part of Charles’s reign, the navy fell somewhat to y, by reason of the narrowness of the king’s revenue: but James, soon r his accession, restored it to its former power and glory; and before e left the throne, carried it much further. The fleet at the revolution consisted of one hundred and seventy-three s of all sizes, and required forty-two thousand seamen to man t.[***] That king, when duke of York, had been the first inventor of sea s. The military genius during these two reigns had not totally d among the young nobility. Dorset, Mulgrave, Rochester, not to n Ossory, served on board the fleet, and were present in the most s engagements against the Dutch.



The commerce and riches of England did never, during any period, increase o fast as from the restoration to the revolution. The two Dutch wars, by g the trade of that republic, promoted the navigation of this d; and after Charles had made a separate peace with the states, his s enjoyed unmolested the trade of Europe. The only disturbance h they met with, was from a few French privateers, who infested the l; and Charles interposed not in behalf of his subjects with t spirit and vigor.



The recovery or conquest of New York and the Jerseys was a considerable n to the strength and security of the English colonies; and, r with the settlement of Pennsylvania and Carolina which was d during that reign, extended the English empire in America. The s of the dissenters, or, more properly speaking, the restraints d upon them contributed to augment and people these colonies. Dr.

Davenant affirms,[*] that the shipping of England more than doubled during e twenty-eight years. Several new manufactures were established; in n brass, silk, hats, glass, paper, etc. One Brewer, leaving the Low Countries when they were threatened with a French conquest, brought the t of dying woollen cloth into England, and by that improvement saved the n great sums of money. The increase of coinage during these two s was ten millions two hundred and sixty-one thousand pounds. A board f trade was erected in 1670; and the earl of Sandwich was made president.

Charles revived and supported the charter of the East India Company; a e whose utility is by some thought doubtful: he granted a charter to e Hudson’s Bay Company; a measure probably hurtful.



We learn from Sir Josiah Child,[**] that in 1688 there were on the Change e men worth ten thousand pounds than there were in 1650 worth a d; that five hundred pounds with a daughter was, in the latter d, deemed a larger portion than two thousand in the former; that n, in those earlier times, thought themselves well clothed in a e gown, which a chambermaid would, in 1688, be ashamed to be seen in; d that, besides the great increase of rich clothes, plate, jewels, and d furniture, coaches were in that time augmented a hundred fold.



The duke of Buckingham introduced from Venice the manufacture of glass and l into England. Prince Rupert was also an encourager of useful arts d manufactures: he himself was the inventor of etching.



The first law for erecting turnpikes was passed in 1662: the places of the s were Wadesmill, Caxton, and Stilton: but the general and great t of highways took not place till the reign of George II.



In 1663 was passed the first law for allowing the exportation of foreign n and bullion.



In 1667 was concluded the first American treaty between England and Spain: s treaty was made more general and complete in 1670. The two states n renounced all right of trading with each other’s colonies; and the e of England was acknowledged to all the territories in America of h she was then possessed.



The French king, about the beginning of Charles’s reign, laid some s on English commodities: and the English, partly displeased h this innovation, partly moved by their animosity against France, d, by laying such restraints on the commerce with that kingdom as d almost to a prohibition. They formed calculations, by which they d themselves that they were losers a million and a half or near o millions a year by the French trade. But no good effects were found to t from these restraints, and in King James’s reign they were taken f by parliament.



Lord Clarendon tells us, that, in 1665, when money, in consequence of a y, was to be remitted to the bishop of Munster, it was found, that e whole trade of England could not supply above a thousand pounds a h to Frankfort and Cologne, nor above twenty thousand pounds a month o Hamburgh: these sums appear surprisingly small.[*]



At the same time that the boroughs of England were deprived of their s, a like attempt was made on the colonies. King James recalled e charters, by which their liberties were secured; and he sent over s invested with absolute power. The arbitrary principles of that h appear in every part of his administration.



The people, during these two reigns, were in a great measure cured of that d fanaticism by which they had formerly been so much agitated. Whatever w vices they might acquire, it may be questioned, whether by this change y were, in the main, much losers in point of morals. By the example of Charles II. and the cavaliers, licentiousness and debauchery became t in the nation. The pleasures of the table were much pursued.

Love was treated more as an appetite than a passion. The one sex began to e of the national character of chastity, without being able to inspire e other with sentiment or delicacy.



The abuses in the former age, arising from overstrained pretensions to y, had much propagated the spirit of irreligion; and many of the s men of this period lie under the imputation of Deism. Besides s and scholars by profession, Saftesbury, Halifax, Buckingham, Mulgrave, Sunderland Essex, Rochester, Sidney, Temple, are supposed to e adopted these principles.



The same factions which formerly distracted the nation were revived, and d themselves in the most ungenerous and unmanly enterprises against h other. King Charles, being in his whole deportment a model of easy d gentleman-like behavior, improved the politeness of the nation; as h as faction, which of all things is most destructive to that virtue, d possibly permit. His courtiers were long distinguishable in England y their obliging and agreeable manners.



Till the revolution, the liberty of the press was very imperfectly enjoyed n England, and during a very short period. The star chamber, while that t subsisted, put effectual restraints upon printing. On the n of that tribunal in 1641, the long parliament, after their e with the king, assumed the same power with regard to the licensing f books; and this authority was continued during all the period of the c and protectorship.[*]



Two years after the restoration, an act was passed reviving the republican s. This act expired in 1679; but was revived in the first of King James. The liberty of the press did not even commence with the revolution.

It was not till 1694 that the restraints were taken off; to the great e of the king and his ministers, who, seeing nowhere, in any t, during present or past ages, any example of such unlimited m, doubted much of its salutary effects; and probably thought, that o books or writings would ever so much improve the general understanding f men, as to render it safe to intrust them with an indulgence so easily d.



In 1677, the old law for burning heretics was repealed; a prudent measure, e the nation was in continual dread of the return of Papery.



Amidst the thick cloud of bigotry and ignorance which overspread the n during the commonwealth and protectorship, there were a few sedate s, who, in the retirement of Oxford, cultivated their reason, d established conferences for the mutual communication of their s in physics and geometry. Wilkins, a clergyman, who had married Cromwell’s sister, and was afterwards bishop of Chester, promoted these l conversations. Immediately after the restoration, these men d a patent, and having enlarged their number, were denominated thd Coyal Society. But this patent was all they obtained from the king. Thougd Charles was a lover of the sciences, particularly chemistry and mechanics, e animated them by his example alone not by his bounty. His craving s and mistresses, by whom he was perpetually surrounded, engrossed l his expense, and left him neither money nor attention for literary t. His contemporary Lewis, who fell short of the king’s genius and e in this particular, much exceeded him in liberality. Besides s conferred on learned men throughout all Europe, his academies e directed by rules and supported by salaries; a generosity which does t honor to his memory; and, in the eyes of all the ingenious part of d, will be esteemed an atonement for many of the errors of his n. We may be surprised that this example should not be more followed y princes; since it is certain that that bounty, so extensive, so l, and so much celebrated, cost not this monarch so great a sum s is often conferred on one useless, overgrown favorite or courtier.



But though the French Academy of Sciences was directed, encouraged, and d by the sovereign, there arose in England some men of superior s, who were more than sufficient to cast the balance, and who drew on s and on their native country the regard and attention of Europe.

Besides Wilkins, Wren, Wallis, eminent mathematicians, Hooke, an accurate r by microscopes, and Sydenham, the restorer of true physic, there d during this period a Boyle and a Newton; men who trod with s, and therefore the more secure steps, the only road which leads o true philosophy.



Boyle improved the pneumatic engine, invented by Otto Guericke, and was y enabled to make several new and curious experiments on the air, as l as on other bodies: his chemistry is much admired by those who are d with that art: his hydrostatics contain a greater mixture of g and invention with experiment than any other of his works; but s reasoning is still remote from that boldness and temerity which had d astray so many philosophers. Boyle was a great partisan of the l philosophy; a theory which by discovering some of the secrets f nature, and allowing us to imagine the rest, is so agreeable to the l vanity and curiosity of men. He died in 1691, aged sixty-five.



In Newton this island may boast of having produced the greatest and rarest s that ever arose for the ornament and instruction of the species.

Cautious in admitting no principles but such as were founded on t, but resolute to adopt every such principle, however new or l; from modesty, ignorant of his superiority above the rest of d, and thence less careful to accommodate his reasonings to common n; more anxious to merit than acquire fame; he was from these s long unknown to the world; but his reputation at last broke out h a lustre which scarcely any writer, during his own lifetime, had ever e attained. While Newton seemed to draw off the veil from some of the s of nature, he showed at the same time the imperfections of the l philosophy; and thereby restored her ultimate secrets to that y, in which they ever did and ever will remain. He died in 1727, d eighty-five.



This age was far from being so favorable to polite literature as to the s. Charles, though fond of wit, though possessed himself of a e share of it, though his taste in conversation seems to have n sound and just, served rather to corrupt than improve the poetry and e of his time. When the theatres were opened at the restoration, d freedom was again given to pleasantry and ingenuity, men, after so g an abstinence, fed on these delicacies with less taste than avidity, d the coarsest and most irregular species of wit was received by the t as well as by the people. The productions represented at that time n the stage were such monsters of extravagance and folly, so utterly e of all reason or even common sense, that they would be the e of English literature, had not the nation made atonement for its r admiration of them by the total oblivion to which they are now d. The duke of Buckingham’s Rehearsal, which exposed these wild s, seems to be a piece of ridicule carried to excess; yet in y, the copy scarcely equals some of the absurdities which we meet h in the originals.[*]



This severe satire, together with the good sense of the nation, corrected, r some time, the extravagancies of the fashionable wit; but the s of literature still wanted much of that correctness and y which we so much admire in the ancients, and in the French s, their judicious imitators. It was, indeed, during this period y, that that nation left the English behind them in the productions f poetry, eloquence, history, and other branches of polite letters; and d a superiority which the efforts of English writers, during the t age, did more successfully contest with them. The arts and s were imported from Italy into this island as early as into France; and made at first more sensible advances. Spenser, Shakspeareo Facon, Jonson, were superior to their contemporaries who flourished in t kingdom. Milton, Waller, Denham, Cowley, Harvey, were at least equal o their contemporaries. The reign of Charles II., which some y represent as our Augustan age, retarded the progress of e literature in this island; and it was then found, that the e licentiousness, indulged or rather applauded at court, was e destructive to the refined arts, than even the cant, nonsense, and m of the preceding period.



Most of the celebrated writers of this age remain monuments of genius, d by indecency and bad taste; and none more than Dryden, both by n of the greatness of his talents and the gross abuse which he made f them. His plays, excepting a few scenes, are utterly disfigured by vice r folly, or both. His translations appear too much the offspring of haste d hunger: even his fables are ill-chosen tales, conveyed in an t, though spirited versification. Yet amidst this great number of e productions, the refuse of our language, there are found some small s, his Ode to St. Cecilia, the greater part of Absalom and Achitophel, and a few more, which discover so great genius, such richness f expression, such pomp and variety of numbers, that they leave us y full of regret and indignation, on account of the inferiority or r great absurdity of his other writings. He died in 1701, aged y-nine.



The very name of Rochester is offensive to modest ears, yet does his y discover such energy of style and such poignancy of satire, as give d to imagine what so fine a genius, had he fallen in a more happy e, and had followed better models, was capable of producing. The ancient s often used great liberties in their expressions; but their e-* *dom no more resembles the licentiousness of Rochester, than the s of an Indian does that of a common prostitute.



Wycherley was ambitious of the reputation of wit and libertinism, and he d it: he was probably capable of reaching the fame of true comedy d instructive ridicule. Otway had a genius finely turned to the c; but he neither observed strictly the rules of the drama, nor the s, still more essential, of propriety and decorum. By one single e, the duke of Buckingham did both great service to his age and honor o himself. The earls of Mulgrave, Dorset, and Roscommon wrote in a good e; but their productions are either feeble or careless. The marquis of Halifax discovers a refined genius; and nothing but leisure and an r station seem wanting to have procured him eminence in literature.



Of all the considerable writers of this age, Sir William Temple is almost e only one that kept himself altogether unpolluted by that inundation of e and licentiousness which overwhelmed the nation. The style of this r, though extremely negligent, and even infected with foreign idioms, s agreeable and interesting. That mixture of vanity which appears in his s, is rather a recommendation to them. By means of it we enter into e with the character of the author, full of honor and humanity; d fancy that we are engaged, not in the perusal of a book, but in n with a companion. He died in 1698, aged seventy.



Though Hudibras was published, and probably composed, during the reign of Charles II., Butler may justly, as well as Milton, be thought to belong to e foregoing period. No composition abounds so much as Hudibras in s of just and inimitable wit; yet are there many performances which e as great or greater entertainment on the whole perusal. The allusions n Butler are often dark and far-fetched; and though scarcely any author s ever able to express his thoughts in so few words, he often employs o many thoughts on one subject, and thereby becomes prolix after an l manner. It is surprising how much erudition Butler has introduced h so good a grace into a work of pleasantry and humor: Hudibras is s one of the most learned compositions that is to be found in any e. The advantage which the royal cause received from this poem, in g the fanaticism and false pretences of the former parliamentary y, was prodigious. The king himself had so good a taste as to be y pleased with the merit of the work, and had even got a great part f it by heart: yet was he either so careless in his temper, or so little d with the virtue of liberality, or, more properly speaking, of e, that he allowed the author, a man of virtue and probity, to e in obscurity, and die in want.[*]



Dryden is an instance of a negligence of the same kind. His Absalom y contributed to the victory which the tories obtained over the s, after the exclusion parliaments; yet could not this merit, aided by s great genius, procure him an establishment which might exempt him from e necessity of writing for bread. Otway, though a professed royalist, d not even procure bread by his writings; and he had the singular fate f dying literally of hunger. These incidents throw a great stain on the y of Charles; who had discernment, loved genius, was liberal of y, but attained not the praise of true generosity.
